Subject:   Project Performance Audit Report:
Manila Urban Development Project
(Loans 1272-PH/1282-PH) and
Second Urban Development Project
(Loan 1647-PH)
Dear Mr. Nowicki:
Thank you for sending us a copy of the abovecaptioned report
and for allowing us to give our camments. While we concur in general
with the findings of the PPAR, we have a few comments on the report
as follows:
On Implementation Performance
Overall, the report offers no new lessons but reiterates the
same lessons learned from the implementation experience of past
projects, i.e., the need for thorough and adequate project prepara-
tion especially in the case of the subject project, which was experi-
mental in nature and the need for the executing agency to have
adequate institutional capability to carry out the project. It
appears high time for both the government and the Bank to place
emphasis on these aspects in future projects.
In addition, to the 'technical'factors that accounted for the
poor implementatior. performance of the project, we note that the
experimental nature of the project, as well as the sensitive issues
involved, e.g., relocation of squatters, necessitate a more careful
consideration of local situations and culture.
On Project Results
We note with disappointment the report's lack of focus on the
project's socio-economic results. It would have been more worth-
while if the report considered not only the achivement of physical



36                     ATTACHMENT A
Page 2 of 2
target but those of socio-economic outcomes of the project,
particularly on addressing inequalities in the distribution
of urban services in support of the urban poor, improvement
in the quality of life and effects on beneficiaries' attitudes
and behaviors.
Relatedly, the issue of cost recovery should be seriously
looked into. The report mentions the lack of political will
and technical capacity of local governments for collection of
mortgage installments. While the latter may be true, afford-
ability by target beneficiaries (e.g., urban poor) should also be
considered. It is noted that the targetted cross-subsidy assump-
tions made at appraisal did not materialize on account of low
sales of commercial and industrial lots. The risks involved with
cross-subsidies should have been foreseen at appraisal, and the
level of anticipated risk should have been incorporated in rate
of return analysis.
It would also be helpful if estimates of financial internal
rates of return could be presented in the final report.
On Maintenance of Project Assets
We agree with the Bank's observation on the need to expedite
operation and maintenance arrangements for the project to sustain
the positive results that have been generated. In this respect,
maximum participation by the beneficiary commmities in operation
and maintenance should be explored.
We hope that our comments will be useful in the finalization
of the PPAR.
Very truly yours,
SOLITA COLLAS-MONSOD
Secretary of Socio-Economic Planning &
Director-General



THE WORLD BANKIINTERNATIONAL FNANCE CORPORATION  37
OFFICE MEMORANDUM EAST ASIA & PACIFIC REGIONAL OFFICE PCR
DATE      December 31, 1986
TO      Mr. D. Ahmad, Acting Chief, AEPUW
FROM      D. Haldane, AE      f
EXTENSION    61931
SUBJFCT     PHILIPPINES: Second Urban Development Project
Project Completion Report (PCR)
1.   Attached is the PCR on the above project prepared by the National Housing
Authority (NHA). This memo supplements the PCR in order to:
(a) highlightsome of the more important issues and lessons of Urban II
and add the Bank's perspective;
(b) cover points omitted from NHA's report; and
(c) update the reader on the status of recommendations made in the PCR.
2.   The attached report attempts to minimize duplication of material covered
under the PCR on Urban I issued last April 11, 1996, on which little, if any,
change has occurred. It -focuses therefore mainly on features specific to
Urban II or requiring follow up in the Urban I PCR. The environmental context
and institutional assessments of the NHA's first ten years will be found in
the Urban I PCR. Thus for a full picture of the NHA's Bank-assisted program,
both reports should be taken together.
3.   The central new feature of Urban II was the introduction of the agency's
first regional operations, and one of the project's most important lessons
related to NHA's unsatisfactory experience with joint ventures with the local
governments. Recommendations for revised institutional arrangements for
future national shelter development have played an important part in the
shelter sector rationalization, the first stage of which was recently signed
into law by President Aquino. Thus, despite serious problems encountered
under Urban II with regionally dispersed operations, the project experience
has usefully contributed to what will hopefully be a more rational and
efficient shelter delivery network in future.
Key Issues
Issue I: Institutional Arrangements
4.   The project's financial arrangements placed NHA in the de-facto position
of a municipal lending bank, with NHA making loans to local governments which
originate,   hold and collecting on the mortgage assets built.          Local
governments, however, proved to be unstable partners, with Cagayan withdrawing
from the project shortly after local elections installed a new mayor. Much
time was lost to rebuilding the project's momentum. In addition, sites were
turned over to local governments following physical completion, making cities
responsible for sales and collections. A combination of poor administration



38
and lack of political will has resulted in negligible cost reco,very on the
regional component as outlined in para 6 below.
5.   This experience helped crystalize the decision that local governments
represented an inappropriate institutional base for the management of
financial instruments such as mortgages. Particularly so for serviced sites
where participation is voluntary and eviction for non payment both possible
and necessary. The question is less clear for upgrading where participation
is largely but not entirely voluntary, and eviction extremely difficult. While
many mortgages in upgraded areas are viable, a proportion are not. The
appropriate institutional home for such relatively uncollectible paper is
still under discussion. However, recommendations that NHA disengage from
joint financial (mortgage) ventures with local governments have been
implemented, and the Bank's Urban IV project, which contained such provisions,
is being restructured to place 100% of the financial responsibility with NHA.
Local governments continue to play important roles in nonfinancial matters.
Issue II: NHA's Debt Crisis/(Cost Recovery/Sales)
6.  Collections were just underway at the time of the Urban I PCR, and only a
year has passed since then. However, further work has been done on the
combined (compounded) impact of deficiencies in the Authority's pricing
structure, sales performance and collection efficiency. Tables I and II
present the compounded effect of these three problems on overall cost
recovery. Actual recovery on residential units at the time of writing is
about 15% vs. 75% estimated at appraisal. For commercial/industrial units the
comparable figure is 11% vs roughly 110% estimated at appraisal. A significant
and unforeseen proportion of this gap is due to slow sales, meaning document
signature which enables startup of repayment. At appraisal, immediate sale of
all lots was anticipated. However, in reality titles appear to have been
awarded in large politically motivated events without simultaneous signature
of the mortgage document. Thus many more lots are reported as awarded than
are "sold" in the sense of signed and under collection. Sales in upgraded
areas are particularly troubled with only 17% of completed units sold as of
June, 1986. Obtaining signature is difficult on completed and occupied
upgrading sites and evicting occupants who have not yet signed is difficult.
For serviced sites, only 56% of completed units are sold (under collection)
but 68% are reported as allocated. It is not clear if the remaining one third
are illegally occupied. It appears both NHA and the cities lack the political
will to enforce eviction in serviced site areas on unsigned units. (Table III
summarizes sales as of June, 1986.) A diagnostic study is needed to check this
data, pinpoint the bottlenecks and lay out a program for resolving NHA's sales
problem.
7.   This lethal combination of institutional weaknesses triggered NHA's debt
crisis which threatens the institutions very survival. The Authority's debt
service ratio was 0.25 in 1985 and has further deteriorated in 1986. Sales of
existing assets and annual budget allocations are helping bridge the gap until
new policies and procedures can be put in place to rectify the situation.
8.   The Authority is aware of these problems, and some progress is being made
in resolving them. Specific recommendations and their status are listed
below:



39
With regard to pricing:
(a) costs of both on-site and off-site water, sewerage and roads
infrastructure previously charged off to other agencies (subsequently
not accepted under both Urban I and II) will in future be charged to
beneficiaries and included in the mortgage unless a signed
contractual agreement exists stipulating the amount and terms of
payment to NHA by another agency. In the latter case, NHA will
function as a contractor to the owner agency. As a result, unit costs
could double in future projects. The MWSS indicates that it has
refused to accept water and sewerage works because it's pricing
structure is inadequate to cover the associated costs. Thus charging
beneficiaries would not result in double charging.
(b) construction in future will not begin until NHA holds title to a
property and therefore knows the acquisition price;
(c) administrative costs and interest during construction will be added
to the sale price, both of which were omitted from Urban I and II;
and
(d) a 20% "default surcharge" has been added to the base cost.
The new pricing policy is already being implemented in the Pasig site of
Urban III.
With regard to sales:
(a) in future it is proposed that NHA function as a developer, selling
its mortgages to a financial institution.      Thus sales must be
complete before NHA can obtain payment for its mortgage. This should
create a strong incentive to execute sales immediately upon
completion, if not before. Since land title will be in hand before
construction, lack of title would no longer delay sales;
(b) a system of pre-sales combined with downpayments is to be tried out
under which lottery winners will begin savings accounts to build a
downpayment (10% agreed; 20% recommended by the Bank). Deposits of
those unable to meet the downpayment requirement after the allowed
period will be refunded together with earned interest, or the
application may be extended until the next lottery;
(b) the practice of title award without simultaneous document signature
must be immediately terminated.
With regard to collection efficiency:
(a) as noted above, NHA will no longer collect under future arrangements.
Collections will be handled by a financial institution with a private
Bank interface between the public agency holding the mortgage and the
individual mortgagee. However, it is possible that as long as public
agencies hold loan assets, payment may be poor in view of the relaxed
payment culture existing in the Philippines which is well described
in the attached PCR. Much depends on the financial agency's



40
administrative efficiency and willingness to enforce sanctions; and
(b) loan loss reserves must be established by all institutions holding
uninsured mortgages. COA has concurred in this recommendation which
is to be implemented in 1987 for NHMFC and 1986 for NHA.
9.  If all the above recommendations are implemented, cost recovery on
residential units could theoretically rise from the present 15% to 103%, as
shown in Table I assuming 95% sales and 90% collection efficiency. The
pricing changes are already in effect, and the Authority is in the process of
shifting to a policy of not initiating construction until land has been
acquired and the price is therefore known. These two policy shifts represent
fundamental and quite difficult changes. However, tightening sales procedures,
enforcing sanctions and cleaning up the unsold (undocumented) inventory
backlog represent several of NHA's most compelling and difficult tasks,
particularly under the present political climate. The task is complicated in
Urban II by the local governments' ownership of the sites in Davao and Cebu.
These may simply have to be written off to experience. The institution's very
survival will depend upon its success in addressing these procedural and
financial issues.
Issue III: Turnover
10.  The Urban I PCR noted NHA's difficulties in turning over infrastructure
completed under both projects to MWSS, MPWH and others. As noted in this PCR,
the MPWH has now accepted all completed works for maintenance purposes, though
the financial asset transfer has not taken place. The MWSS, however, remains
unwilling to accept works and the legal documents under both Urban I and II
are vague on this important point. As a result, NHA remains at least formally
responsible for servicing the associated debt. In future, legal documents
should clearly stipulate which agency ultimately will own the asset built and
bear the associated debt burden. Due to this gap in project documentation, it
was necessary to obtain a Cabinet approval and Presidential directive, signed
December 18th, clarifying maintenance responsibilities and directing MWSS and
others to accept completed infrastructure. Though the matter is therefore in
principle resolved, the directive has yet to be implemented and will doubtless
take time to execute.
11. Under the regional cities component of Urban II, an experimental approach
was tried out with MPWH, MWSS and others directly building their own off-site
infrastructure. While this successfully averted the turnover problem, it
predictably generated difficulties of coordination and timing of these works
to dovetail with NHA's needs. Until sites are functional, they cannot be
occupied and collections started. Having now tried both approaches, the NHA
has concluded that self-contained projects are preferable, and that the extra
costs must, as noted above, be added to the mortgage. Standards will be
trimmed back to meet affordability constraints.
Issue IV: Cross-Subsidies
12. Both Urban I and II entailed substantial cross-subsidies, utilizing
projected profits from commercial/industrial (CI) lots to subsidize prices on
the residential units. These proved unworkable in both projects, and have
contributed significantly to NHA's current debt service crisis. The essential



41
weakness in the cross-subsidy theory is that it ignores business cycles, and
differential demand for residential and commercial/industrial properties. A
steady demand curve was assumed at appraisal lasting through completion, a
period anticipated at four years and spanning in reality 7 years (Table IV).
The worldwide economic recession of the 1980-81 period, followed by the 1982-
83 crisis flattened demand for CI property, and eroded affordability on
residential units. Demand moved downward toward cheaper units such as NHA was
building. Accompanying high inflation in 1984 and 1985 as sites were completed
depressed economic expansion, but stimulated demand for inexpensive
residential real estate as a classic store of value in inflationary periods.
Some sales also occurred in CI property, but this represents a far less
liquid, longer-term investment prospect. Thus NHA encountered heavy demand for
the subsidized residential units (many of which are occupied though not
documented) and quite depressed demand for the Cl units which were to provide
the requisite profit. The Authority's inventory value will doubtless keep pace
with inflation if it can afford to hold the investment until market conditions
improve, but inventory contributes nothing to NHA's acute cash needs for debt
servicing.
13. Commercial/industrial properties in the regional cities are a particular
problem, with only about 5% of the existing inventory sold. As a result,
proposed CI investments under Urban IV have been deferred until the existing
inventory is reduced, and demand picks up.
14. Experience under both Urban I and II has therefore forced NHA to the
conclusion that internal cross-subsidies are excessively risky, and cannot be
relied upon to provide cash when needed. Borrowings secured against these and
other properties have reached a maximum prudent limit. The business
environment on which these arrangements rely is too cyclic, volatile, and
unpredictable for NHA to risk its institutional viability. The Bank fully
concurs in NHA's judgement. It is in fact, our view that the private sector
should undertake commercial/industrial property development, and that NHA
should disengage from this type of activity. The new Executive Order on
rationalization of the housing sector appears to approve this change, but the
language is not explicit and a clarification is needed.
Issue V: Business Loans
15. Urban II included a small business loan (SBL) component which was to be
implemented by a Bank, though firm arrangements had not been agreed at the
time of appraisal and were left to be worked out during implementation. In
actuality, the two banks identified at appraisal declined the role and NHA
ultimately implemented the component.     Experience with SBL's has been
exceptionally poor. Only a third of the amount earmarked was taken up, and
collections total only 20% of the amount due. Though NHA is committed to the
program which is politically popular, business lending has not been
professionally managed and has become blurred with the Authority's social
objectives. Ultimately the success of any business lending program must be
judged by whether the funds are recycled into a replicable program or become a
grant to the initial loan recipient. By this criteria the component must be
judged a failure. The Bank has recommended to Government that a specialized
institution be named into which such programs could be transferred, and that
NHA terminate further business lending. The new Executive Order concurs in
this mandate directive.



42
Issue VI: Resettlement
16. Urban I and II focused largely on establishing a national upgrading
program which, as noted in the NHA's PCR, brought about an important
modification in a 30 year old Government policy of squatter relocation. It did
not, however, terminate relocation which has continued to dominate an
excessive amount of NHA's institutional capacity. Of the approximately
133,000 units built by NHA in its first ten years, only 6,000 to 10,000 (or a
maximum of about 7%) have contributed to expanding the housing stock. All of
these were under Urban II and III. The 10,000 new low cost units served less
than 2% of the demand from new growth alone estimated at between 60,000 to
80,000 units per year in the market segment serviced exclusively by NHA and
the informal sector. Thus squatting pressures in fact intensified over the
decade. Production of new housing stock must be significantly expanded. While
the NHA's new six year program should reflect this shift, private sector
participation is ultimately the only means capable of delivering the volume
needed, and the Housing Sector Loan is intended to help develop the necessary
mechanisms to achieve this goal.
Issue VII: Flood Control
17. The PCR correctly points out that the project's modest contribution of
additional equipment and TA did not resolve the flooding problems of Manila
which have, in fact, grown more severe. A comprehensive review of the causes
of flooding in Manila is needed along with a project focused on resolving
these problems. Such a project is under discussion between the Government and
the Bank. A separate PCR report prepared by MPWH is available on file
detailing results of the flood control component.
Issue VIII: Land Acquisition
18. Land costs were significantly underestimated at appraisal, and land
acquisition problems plagued the project throughout implementation. Appraisal
estimates were based on the assumption that the prices frozen under the Areas
for Priority Development (APD) Order would prevail. In actuality,
expropriation took years and in many cases is still unresolved. Despite
protracted litigation in the courts, prices were finally set far closer to
market than expected. This enabled NHA to get on with s 4%division and
titling. For years lack of land title prevented finalization of  t sales as a
Deed of Sale with Mortgage was being utilized requiring title. Thus lack of
title resulted in NHA's inability to document the sale and initiate
collections. In 1984 the NHA shifted to a Conditional Contract of Sale in
which title was not required, thus removing legal obstacles to completing
sales and starting collections. A further and more important policy shift is
also taking place under which the Authority will now undertake construction of
a site only after title is acquired and the final price known. Adjusting to
this new policy will be quite difficult, with several years of intensive land
acquisition and little construction, but over the longer term is expected to
yield substantial dividends.
Issue IX: Disbursements
19. A learning curve is clearly evident in the contrast between the Urban I



43
disbursement profile and the Urban II profile (Chart V). The first project
closely paralleled the overall urban profile, but the second significantly
improved on it, cutting over two years from the profile. The fact the project
did not achieve the highly optimistic appraisal schedule (of substantively 3
years) should not be allowed to detract from this Improvement.
Issue X: Economic Evaluation
20. The 19% return noted in NHA's revaluation is probably quite optimistic
and suffers from some methodological constraints inherent in the nature of the
instrument rather than the method or data utilized by NHA. The evaluation
utilizes "market" values of commercial/industrial property based on a few
sporadic sales in an otherwise highly depressed market in which little
movement was occurring. As noted above, even the Authority's own CI property
remains largely unsold to date. The market's general suppression may have been
partly mitigated by demand generated by speculative buyers seeking inflation
hedges during this period, especially for residential properties. However,
the reliability of the data as a true judge of the market may be arguable, and
the economic rate of return similarly affected.
Issue XI: NHA's Financial Management
21.  The Authority's financial management and performance remains weak -- a
protracted problem which virtually endangers the agency's survival. Loans
under Urban III will severely strain NHA's debt service capacity and dramatic
steps will be needed to rectify the pricing, sales and collections problems
now depressing NHA's cash generation if the Authority is to regain its
capacity to again borrow and repay external loans.
22. In conclusion, the project achieved its key objective of nationalizing
the upgrading and serviced site program. However the institutional model
utilized (NHA loans to local governments) proved unreplicable. The projectbs
failures (notably the joint financial ventures with local governments, and the
performance of small business loans, home material loans and cross subsidies
have contributed to both the NHA's and the Bank's understanding of the
sectoral institutions strengths and weaknesses, and key issues leading to the
development of the Housing Sector Rationalization Plan, the first stage of
which was approved in December, 1986. It is hoped that with the adoption of a
significant proportion of the policy agenda under this Plan, many of the
problems encountered under both Urban I and II will be rectified, and the
sector placed on a sounder policy and financial footing to achieve its longer
term objectives.

PHILIPPINES
Second Urban Development Project (Loan 1647,PH)
PROJECT COMPLETION REPORT
Highlights
1.  The project was set against a background of severe service
deficiencies and rapid growth of squatter settlements in urban centers
throughout the country. More than 25% of the population in major
regional cities were living in dense central sites, usually located
on foreshore areas, under exceptionally poor sanitary conditions.
Population growth contihued at more than 5% per annum (p.a.) in Manila,
and regional development was clearly needed to relieve pressure on
the primate city.
2.  The prQject included the Third phase of development at the
Dagat-Dagatan sites and services area in Manila and initiated the
national upgrading and serviced sites program in three key regional
cities. The basic thrust of the First Urban Development Project (Loan
1272T/1282-PH) was continued, aiming to provide low-income urban resi-
dents with secure tenure and basic services for which they were to
repay over 25 years costs of land and on-plot and on-site development.
The project was the first effort of Government through the National
Housing Authority (NHA) to establish a national shelter program in
concert with local governments outside of Manila.   The project also
intended to spur ecoromic activity and employment opportunities through
a small business loan component and the development of three associated
commercial/industrial areas. Additional objectives included acquisi-
tion of flood control equipment for Manila and technical assistance to.
a variety of agencies.
3. The project design and implementation. period spanned 7.5 years
rather than the five-year schedule optimistically projected at appraisal.
However, actual construction, once begun, required little more than
5.5 years. Disbursements were substantially complete Within 7 years.-
4. Delays were attributable to technical problems at Dagat-Dagatan,
slow land acquisition, and slow release of Government counterpart funds.
The organizational and logistical challenges of establishing-new projects
in the regional cities were also significant.
5. Total costs for the main housing component of the project were
P689.12 M (excluding interim interest), a 45% increase over the apprai-
sal estimate. Much of this increase was attributed to the cost of land
and reclamation, which rose by 362% over appraisal estimates.- Never-
theless, because of the declining value of the peso during the project
period there was a 1.5% dollar decrease in total project cost.
($62.65 M actual at P11 = $1, v_s_. $6.5 M appraised at P7.5 = $1).



52
6. Increased casts did not affect the project's affordability as
incomes also rose. Average lots in all proJect areas are afforded by
households with incomes at or below the 20th percentile of the respective
income distributions.
7. Affordability was maintained through internal subsidies as a
result of differential pricing for residential lots and projected commer-
cial/industrial sales. C/I sales are of great importance, comprising
52% of estimated revenues against 57% at appraisal.   Sales have, however,
been disappointing in two of the three C/I areas, and improved marketing
efforts are needed if profitability is to be achieved.   Nevertheless,
taken overall, profitability can only be maintained if there are also
substantial reimbursements and/or assumption of liabilities by concerned
agencies for works undertaken by NHA on their behalf which amount to
41% of total investments.
8. Serious cost recovery efforts were initiated in early 1984 when
government was faced with an economic crisis in 1983 when NHA had to
rely on its collections to sustain its operations. Faced with a commu-
nity unaccustomed to paying charges for the lots awarded to then, NHA
embarked on a cost recovery information program throughout its project
areas. As a result, NHA's overall collections increased by 56% from
P100.99 M in 1984 to P158.11 M in 1985. Collection efficiency in Bank-
assisted projects was higher than that for locally-funded projects with
69% collected in 1985 increasing from 54%'in 1986. The serious attention
given by NHA to its recovery program has been commendable.- Improved
monitoring and control has been reflected in performance and recovery is
being enhanced through an improved rate of disposition for both residential
and commercial areas.
9. Turnover to other agencies of completed infrastructure for main-
tenance purposes has improved from a slow start with MPWH accepting
the major responsibilities during early 1985.   Despite continuing
efforts,problems are still being experienced with MWSS over the acceptance
of comoleted water and sewer works and reimbursement of expenditures from
ctherGovenmeittministries has not taken place. Taken overall these consti-
tutes a large drain on NHA's limited resources. Compounding the problem,
repayment of subsidiary loans by local governments to NHA has not bee.a
satisfactory. In future NHA projects,appropriate measures must be taken
in advance to alleviate these problems.
10. NHA showed remarkable flexibility in dealing with the technical
challenges, dramatic inflation, and a highly politic'ized environment
which characterized the project implementation period. The capacity to
produce affordable units has been demonstrated, and NHA's credibility
as an implementor is well established. Much work remains to be done,
however, on financial performance and coordination with Government
housing finance agencies. NHA's'future is heavily dependent upon in-
creased generation of corporate receipts to finance new projects and
service debt.



53
II. Performance of local govermnents under the project was uneven.
Their role in future efforts to expand the national shelter program is
of critical importance and must be detenmined in concert with tho extent
to which NHA is able to decentralize its operations.
i2.   In conclusion, the project achieved a dramatic improvement in
the living conditions of more than 25,000 low-income Filipino families.
Government policies now formally embrace upgrading and serviced sites
development as more socially acceptable and cost effective than previous
approaches. However in future projects, more attention should be given
towards cost recovery and the "soft ware" aspects of project implement-
ation and management if a truly replicable national program is to take
place.



54
PHILIPPINES
SECOND URBAN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
(LOAN 1647-PH)
PROJECT COMPLETION REPORT
I. CONDITIONS LEADING TO THE PROJECT
1.01 The First Urban Development Project (Urban I) in the Phil-
ippines (Loan No. 1272 T :1282-1-PH) was approvedin May 1976 and con-
centrated mainly upon the improvement of the tondo Foreshore slum area
and the development of the first phase of the adjacent 400 has. Dagat-
Dagatan sites and services project in Manila.
1.02 The Second Urban Development Project (Urban II), the subject
of this report, sought to continue assistance to the Philippine Govern-
ment in developing affordable solutions to problems of poverty, shelter
and environmental sanitation, not only in Manila but in major regional
cities throughout the country. Specifically, the project's objectives
were (SAR, pg.8):
a. Establishment of a national program to improve basic
services and regularize land tenure in major urban
slum areas;
b. Establishment by the National Housing Authority (NHA)
of a nationwide sites and services development program
to make available well located and affordable residential
plots in major urban centers;
c. Promotion of employment through development of commercial
and industrial sites, and provision of business loans
and technical assistance; and
d.  Assistance to NHA and local government to improve
planning and development through an institutional
framework capable of implementing long-tenm, cost
recoverable programs.



55
1.03 A major goal of the project was to involve local governments
directly in upgrading slums and development of new serviced sites within
their areas. -The priority cities of Cagayan de Oro, Cebu and Davao were
identified for inclusion by NHA and its Board of Directors. In Metro-
Manila, the project sought to assist development of the first sites
and services area not tied to a specific relocation program. This deve-
lopment of the third phase of Dagat-Dagatan would not only add to Manila's
housing stock but would also produce a balanced residential and cwmuer-
cial center for the north-central part of the metropolitan area.
1.04 Experience with the Tondo project under Urban I had persuaded
government thata programofin-situ upgrading of slums and development of
serviced sites could provide viable, low-cost solutions to the burgeoning
housing problems of the country's rapidly growing urban population. It
was also clear that development in major regional cities must be pursued
in order to spur local growth and alleviate mounting pressure on Manila.
o 1.05 Thus, beginning in mid-1977, a series of Presidential Letters
of Instruction (LOIs 555, 557 and 686) were issued formally establishing
the nationwide Slum Improvement and Resettlement (SIR) program. The new
laws directed NHA to take the lead role in assisting local governments
to plan, finance, implement and administer cost recoverable upgrading
and serviced sites projects within the framework of long-temn programs.
Guidelines were set-forth regarding regularization of tenure, allocation
and recovery of costs, responsibilities of other government agencies
and establishment of local government housing offices. The LOIs were
truly innovative since they reversed government housing policies enforced
since World War II. The previous approach of outright relocation of
squatters to distant resettlement sites was replaced by a more realistic
and affordable strategy of in-situ upgrading. Overnight the country had,
on paper, a national program to work with low-income urban citizens to
redress basic service inequities. The Second Urban Development Project
was the first attempt to implement this policy on a national scale.
II. THE PROJECT
A. Identification, Preparation and Appraisal
2.01 Project identification was carried out in 1976 and early
1977. In Metro Manila, the third phase of Dagat-Dagatan was identi-
fied as highest priority, following early development of Phases I
and II. Phase I was developed under Urban I with World Bank assist-
ance while Phase II, initially started by NHA, was subsequently
supported through a loan agreement with KFW.signed on April 10,1981.
In addition, high priority was assigned to the acquisition of flood
control equipment for Metro Manila by the Bureau of Public Works
(now MPWH).



56
2.02  Three major regional cities were also identified as
high priority sites for major development:
(i) The central visayan city of Cebu which in
1977 had approximately 25% of its one million
-       population living in slum and squatter areas;
(ii) Davao, the largest city in Mindanao with a
population of about 525,000 in 1977 of whom
22% were living in slum and squatter areas,
with densities up to 1,600 persons per hectare
in several central sites; and
(iii) Cagayan de Oro, located on the northern cost of
Mindanao with a population of 85,000 in 1977
of which 26% were living in slum and squatter
settlements.
2.03 Since under and unemployment were extensive among resi-
dents in slum areas, a high priority in the project was given towards
the promotion of employment and income generation activities through
the development of commercial/industrial land and provision of small
business loans and technical assistance.
2.04 Project preparation started in mid-1977 and foreign
and local consultants were extensively utilized to assist NHA and
the newly created project teams in the regional cities. Pre-appraisal
by the Bank began in late 1977 and was completed in December 1978.
The loan was subsequently approved and became effective in April
1979.
B.  Project Components
2.05 The project was to include the following components;
(SAR, pp. 8-9)
a.  Sites and services in Metro Manila - ($33.57 million
equivalent) at Dagat-Dagatan for about 8,600 residential
plots on about 90 ha. including (i) land, (ii) infrastructure,
(iii) core houses with sanitary facilities, (1v) materials
loans for house completion through self-help, (v) health,
education and social facilities.
b.  Slum imDrovement in the three regional cities of Cebu,
Cagayan de Oro and Davao ($9.31 million eauivalent) in-
vol ving basic infrastructure services and community
facilities for about 7,600 households.-



57
c.  Sites and services in the regional cities ($6.53 million
equivalent) including (i) about 3,800 plots on open land,
(ii) about 800 infill plots in low-density slums and ad-
3acent to high-density slums, (iii) sanitary facilities,
(iv) building materials loans, (v) community facilities.
d. Industrial/commercial development ($9.40 million equiva-
lent) involving 40 ha. of serviced land in Metro Manila
and 15.3 ha. in regional cities for, income generation and
employment purposes.
e. Small business development ($2.19 million equivalent)
including markets, technical assistance and loan funds
for equipment and working capital. In Dagat-Dagatan
approximately 2 ha. and in the regional cities about one
ha. of serviced land was to be provided for cottage
industries integrated within residential areas.
f. Flood control equipment ($2.22 million equivalent) in-
volving purchase of special dredging equipment to control
flooding in Metro Manila.
g. Technical assistance and training ($6.72 million equivalent)
Assistance: to NHA for planning and policy development,
training, project execution, monitoring and feasibility
studies; to MPWTC for flood control studies; to NEDA for
feasibility studies for metropolitan program analysis in
Cebu, Davao and Cagayan de Oro, and for review of national
small scale business programs; to MHS for assistance in
policy and program planning and to the MMC for training
and planning studies.
2.06 Of the total Bank loan of US$32 million equivalent,
US$26.24 million was to be on-lent to NHA. Terms of the loan were
for twenty years including a five-year grace period on principal
repayments with interest at 7.35%. Commitment charges were at 3 1/4%
of 1% computed on the basis of the undrawn balances.
III. MAJOR RESULTS
A. Physical jmpPovements
3.01 All but one of the upgrading project areas were situated
on low lying, swampy coastal sites subject to tidal inundation with
extremely poor drainage and totally inadequate sanitation. As shown
in the accompanying photos, the project achieved dramatic improvements
to the environmental conditions of these areas.



58
3.02 Another major hazard was fire and two upgrading sites
suffered large conflagrations during the project.impleementation  .
period.  Reblocking to provide access to all.ttructures ai.d create
firebreaks as well as the construction of water systems greatly
reduced this hazard that no fires have been experienced in a:y of
the project sites since completioni of construction.-
3.03 Serviced site development at Dagat-Dagatan is depicted
in the accompanying photos, showing how core units prov,ided by the
project were adapted over time by new residents to produce substantial
dwellings. Proper monitoring and    aluation of,residential.improve-
ments has not been pursued but a s..ple study in Davao shows, that
71% of the beneficiaries of upgraded,sites had.invested an average of
P6,000.00 in home improvements during the one.year study period.. As
the photos show, the level of investment Bt-Dagat-Dagatan was obviously
higher. The project clearly demonstrated that substantial private
investment in housing will follow when environmental and infrastructure
improvements are made and tenure.is regularized.
B. Increase in Housing. Stlck   '     -
3.04 Table 3.1 shows the project developed'18,549 residential
lots, about 97% of'the Appraisal estimate. Of-these, more than
11,000 are new serviced lots, the great majority of which were not
linked to relocation.programs. Starting in 1983 in Davao and culmi-
nating in Dagat-Dagatan in late 1984 more than 9,000 of these;lots
were raffled to more than 20,000 prequalified ,landless, low-income
families, an unprecedented.event in'the Phil.1ppines.
C.  Commercial/Industrial-Development      .
3.05  Three major,commercial/industrial. areas were, developed.
under the project encompassing a.gross area.of.82 ha., a 49% increase
over Appraisal estimates.  The 70 has.. net sileable area is valued
at more than P482 million representing 62% of expected revenues.*
3.06  As of December 31, 1985., onl3% of the.commerc  1/indus-
trial area in Cagayan de Oro has been disposed, while in Davao the
figure is 14% and in Dagat-Dagatan 30%. Overall some 54 has. of
saleable land remains undisposed.representing 78% of that completed..
While sales have lagged in the areas of Davao (despite the connmissioning
of a private brokerage firm), Cagayan de Oro and in tile 'ommercial
area of Dagat-Dagatan, sales in the industrial-area of Dagat-Dagatan
are more encouraging with 78% disposed.
D.  Small Business Development
3.07 The project included P16 M to finance a small loans
program intended to create new employment opportunities and increase
incomes of the project beneficiaries. At appraisal;it was assumed



59
that these funds would be administered by Government banks with
assistance from two agencies of the Ministry of Trade and Industry.
These arrangements did not materialize, and the small loans program
was eventually administered directly by NHA in Dagat-Dagatan and
the,local governments in the regional cities.
3.08 Less than one-third of the P16 N available was event-
ually disbursed to beneficiaries (P1.4 M in Dagat-Dagatin; P3.7 M
in Regional Cities). Despite numerous administrative aifficulties
and a low collection efficiency, the program has created over
15,000 jobs in the regional cities at a cost of about P2,000 per
job. In Dagat-Dagatan, the program has benefitted 9,049 households
with P3,648 additional income earned per family per annum. Invest-
ments were mainly for subcontracting and trading ventures.
NHA has remained committed to this program since no other
agency of government is currently providing small business assistance
to the low income urban population.   However,   tighter loan appraisals
and improved collection effqrts are required.
D.  Building Materials Loans
3.09 The project also included about P14 M building materials
loans to promote self-help home improvements but less than one-
third of this amount was utilized (P1.7 M in Dagat-DAgatan; P1.9 M
in regional cities) due to administrative difficulties. Beneficia-
ries appear to have been perfectly capable of acquiring their own
materials from the private sector. The building loans program has
continued with a variety of administrative adjustments under RCDP/
Urban IV but its performance should be more closely monitored and
revised as necessary.
E.  Flood Control Equipment
3.10 The project included $1.9 M equivalent to purchase
flood control equipment for use in Metro Manila. The Metro area
continues, however, to be plaqued by widespread floods every rainy
season.
F. Technical Assistance
3.11 The project included 543 manmonths of technical assistance
for NHA, WEDA, MMC, MHS and MPWH. In addition to project-related
advisory services, NHA also had allocationsto prepare feasibility
.studies for the ZIP in Metro Manila which eventually received Bank
assistance under the Urban III loan.

IV. PROECT IMPLEMENTATION AND COST
A. Start-Up
4.01 Project start-up was delayed by more than one year
due to (1) protracted loan negotiations which culminated in loan
effectivity only in April 1979; (2) dealys in release of govern-
ment counterpart funds to NHA, partially as a result of delayed
loan effectivity; (3) delays in land acquisition which prevented
entrance to various sites and (4) delays in project staff organi-
zation due to lack of funds and difficulties in attracting tech-
nical personnel especially at the local government level.
4.02 The Appraisal estimates; were extremely optimistic in
projecting the start of implementation by mid-1978 considering that
feasibility study preparation began in mid-1977 and the Appraisal
Report itself was finalized only in December 1978 (figure 4.1).
A more realistic projection would have been mid-1979 following loan
effectivity, which was when implementation actually started.
B. Implementation Schedule
4.03 Flexibility and timely management action on specific
problem areas have significantly contributed to the completion of
physical works in Dagat-Dagatan within a 5 1/2 year period despite
initial difficulties. The repackaging of infrastructure works into
smaller contract packages, the establishment of a Special Action
Program to speed up fund releases, the modification in the Bank to
local equity disbursement percentage for the civil works program in
1983 and the crashing of contract schedules are factors which have
contributed to this effort, considering that there were additional
works carried through the program and significant constraints brought
by the construction delays by other agencies. For the Regional Cities
component, Appraisal estimates were very optimistic projecting full
implementation for Davao in three years and only 2 1/2 years for Cebu.
As was the case in Urban T, these overly optimistic ,-ojections
resulted mainly from a lack of actual experience in the regional
cities.
4.04 Dagat-Dagatan - the key feature of Dagat-Dagatan is the
site itself, a 410 has. fish pond which constituted the largest con-
tiguous undeveloped area within urbanized Metro Manila. This 'huge
strategically located site had remained as fishponds precisely
because it was extremenly low-lying, very difficult to drain, full
of silt, and without major road access, water supply, and sanitary
drainage. Predictably, implementation in this difficult site was
delayed for a variety of reasons.



62

63
4.05 As noted in the July 1978 Feasibility Study for Dagat-
Dagatan (Llewelyn-Davies Kinhill Pty. Ltd., pg 25), "the timely
development of the project, if not its feasibility, will be dependent
upon the implementation of major infrastructure components". A major
setback during the initial implementaiton period was the deficient
reclamation undertaken by the then Bureau of Public Works which
necessitated additional bulkearthworks by NHA in 1980, after two years
of futile negotiations with BPWforittoundertake additional filling
required.
Likewise, the then Department of Public Highways (DPH) and
the Metropolitan Water and Sewerage System (MWSS) had existing
and independent programs of work for the construction of major roads,
drains, water systems, all of which were delayed in implementation
due to financial and institutional difficulties. Dagat-Dagatan's
phasing of construction was related to and dependent on the timely
implementation of 2 major circumferential roadways, C-3 and C-4 by
MPH and the permanent water supply by MWSS; these works were only
nearing completion by end of 1985.
4.06  Major drainage works were slowed by poor soil condi-
tions. Roads were delayed until reclamation was complete, and re-
designs were necessary again owing to the poor soil. MWSS water
and sewer specifications were upgraded in 1981 requiring total
redesign including the components of the initial NHA infrastructure
contract which had already been awarded. The economic crisis of
1983-1984 brought a dramatic escalation in construction costs
(See Annex I). The existing governmental contract administration
system (PD 1594'as amended) was incapable of dealing with escalations
of such magnitude and as a result seven contracts at Dagat-Dagatan
were terminated.
4.07 To complicate matters further, the Ministry of Budget
made the first release of government counterpart funds only in
June 1980, more than two years after the projected start of imple-
mentation and even then in inadequate amounts. In October 1983, the
government's disbursement ratio on contract payments was lowered
from 57% to 15% due to its failure to provide the funds as a result
of the financial crisis of 1983. A Special Action Program was ini-
tiated,setting up a revolving fund from which NHA could disburse
foreign funds instead of front ending payments and seeking reimburse-
ments later. Allocation of loan funds were revised to meet the increased
costs of civil works. The pace of implementation quickened consi-
derably to meet the revised closing date of June 1985.
4.08 To summarize, the coordinated implementation of major
infrastructure by other national agencies at Dagat-Dagatan as well
as the timely release of counterpart funds did not occur res'ilting
in.- delays in project implementation. These interagency difficul-
ties are carried over from tie Tondo/Urban I project.   As a fledging



64
government corporation, NHA was in no realistic position to attempt
to coordinate the activities of long established government ministries
which, in any event, have hundreds of other projects and competing
priorities at any given time. The evidence from Urban I and II clearly
indicates that large scale projects such as Dagat-Dagatan which rely
heavily on inputs from several agencies cannot reasonably be expected
to achieve desired implementation schedules.
4.09 Regional Cities - the less complex Regional Cities com-
ponent of the project was delayed for more mundane reasons. First,
all three Cities were reluctant to coanit significant resources to.
the project until they were certain it would materialize, a fact
which was established only in early 1979 following negotiations.
4.10 Second, land to be acquired for the project amounted to
over 108 has. spread across nine sites and involving more than 50
owners in the three cities. NHA's continuing lack of legal staff
and resources combined with the chronically slow provincial judicial
system to produce long delays in acquisition. Land owners appealed
expropriation proceedings right up to the Supreme Court, legally
barring project staff from entering sites even for geodetic surveys.
A writ of possession for the 45 ha. Davao New Matina site, for
instance, was finally secured only in December 1979, almost 1 1/2 years
after the start of implementation estimated at Appraisal.
4.11  Third, relations with prospective beneficiaries in most
upgrading sites were slow to develop. Local governments had no ex-
perience in fostering cooperative arrangements with the upgrading
area Residents who had, up until mid 1977, been the subject of erratic
relocation attempts by government since the Second World War. NHA
could offer little assistance since its community relations staff
was still heavily involved in the Tondo Project. Disastrous fires
in Macabalan (1979) and Piapi (1980) eventually broke the impasse by
providing urgent opportunities for development. As in Tondo, however,
the SAR implementation schedule clearly underestimated the amount
of time and effort, usually at night on weekends, required to elicit
community participation upon which the upgrading projects are so
heavily dependent.
4.12 Fourth, failure of government to release local counter-
part funds in significant amounts until mid-1980 caused further
delays.
4.13 Finally, there were lengthy periods when crucial cons-
truction materials, particularly asphalt and crushed aggregate, were
simply unavailable in the regional cities. Still, with the exception
of the Lapasan project in Cagayan, the regional cities component was
substantially completed in little more than five years (late 1979
to mid-1984) virtually as estimated at Appraisal.



65
C. Costs
4.14 The total cost of the Shelter component is estimated
to be P689.125 M or P214.275 M higher than the appraisal figure of
P474.850 M, an increase of 45%. This estimate is provisional since
precise costs for components constructed by certain line agencies
(for water supply and electricity, community facilities and off-site
works) and for services provided by the cities (pre-development
and some city housing office expenditures) under the SIR program were
not known and estimates were made. In addition, final land acquisi-
tion costs have not been fixed in some projects and again estimates
were made. Table 4.1 compares the costs at completion and appraisal
which are detailed by project and component in Annex III.
4.15 The figures show that the principal cost overruns relate
to reclamation P69.065M) land acquisition (P64.324 M), on-site
infrastructure P62.324 Ml and technical assistance (P39.944 M). A
further P38.337 accounts for the design and supervision cost for
which it is unclear whether or not this was included in the appraisal
estimate. The full impact of these increases was lessened to a
certain extent as a result of reductions in the costs of water supply,
sewerage and with the lowering of on-plot development standards
throughout.
4.16 Under the SIR component, the total cost of P213.163 M
(including technical assistance and small business loans) exceeded
the appraisal estimate of P142.110 M, by 50% with reclamation, land
acquisition, and off-site infrastructure costs accounting for 97%
of the total net increase. It is likely that unrealistic estimates
were made during appraisal and the problems of private land purchase
under estimated. Likewise, the apparent non-recognition of design
and supervision costs at appraisal was on- oversight.   The increase
in technical assistance costs resulted principally from the extended
period that the expatriate engineers-and advisors were needed while
the delay in construcing off-site works is thought to contribute
to the increase in costs of components constructed by MPWH. In-
creases in off-site and reclamation costs were the result of rising
prices for construction materials, as a result of rapid inflation
during the construction period and the need for additional extra -
; works unforeseen at appraisal.
4.17 Similar cost increases were evident at the City and
project level although in Cebu the cost overrun was almost twice
the percentage established in the other cities. This was the only
City where the on-site work costs significantly exceeded those
estimated at appraisal. Land.costs in Davao and Cagayan de Oro,
currently estimated at P24.3 Million and P21.7 Million, greatly
exceeded the appraisal figures of P0.2 Million and P5.3 Million
respectively. Reclamation costs in Cagayan de Oro and off-site
works in Davao too were much greater than expected.

4.18 In Dagat-Dagatan, the total cost of P475.962 M (inclu-
ding technical assistance and small business loans) exceeded the
appraisal estimate of P332.740 M by 43% with actual land acquisition
and reclamation costs accounting for 52% of the cost overrun. No
allowance was made for land cost nor was the need for additional
earthworks foreseen at appraisal. Other major cost overruns are
evident for the drainage component and for roads and footpaths.
The main reasons for the increases were the downturn in the economy,
deficiencies in the earth works originally undertaken by MPWH,
poor subsoil conditions and changes needed to the specifications for
some work items. In addition, the manmonths of technical ssistance
greatly exceeded the appraisal expectations. Savings, however, were
achieved through the lowering of on-plot provision, limiting the
disbursement of building materials and small business loans and
reducing the number of community facilities constructed (only two
elementary schools instead of the.five programmed were constructed).
4.19 The peso was devalued three times during the project
period, and comparison of Appraisal estimAtes made at P7.5 - $1
with actual costs at an average of P11 - $1 reveals a dollar savings
of 1.5% on direct costs and an increase of only 4% on total costs
including interim interest.
4.20 Despite the increases in land and reclamation costs,
NHA minimized overall increases by reducing standards and costs of
development particularly for on-plot items in Dagat-Dagatan. Succeeding
projects, particularly RCDP/Urban IV, have benefitted from the Urban II
experience and now utilize market values to more realistically esti-
mate land costs during project preparation.
4.21  Various calculations of gross development cost for the
304:8 ha. area developed under the project are shown below:

NHA's greatly improved cost control mechanisms under Urban II.



68
D. Disbursements
Of the total loan of $27.94 M, (representing the original
loan of $26.24 M and an additional loan of $1.7 N approved in
1984) about $26.13 M or 94% was disbursed by March, 1986. Disburse-
ments were made over seven (7) years, as opposed to the appraisal
estimate of 4-years. Disbursements were slowed by government's
failure to release counterpart Oinds until mid-1980, but followed
a relatively straight line thereafter with annual disbursements
of about $5 M from 1981 through 1985.
V. FINANCIAL PERFORMANCE
5.01 The key aspects of financial perfomance relate to
cost recovery, demand and affordability, infrastructure turnover
impact on municipal finance and replicability of the national SIR
program.
A. Cost Recovery
5.02 The project aimed to demonstrate the feasibility of
city wide programs that provide services to low income groups with-
out external subsidies. The level of payments afforded by benefi-
ciaries was to be a principal determinant of appropriate standards
and financial terms. Commercial and industrial lots were proposed
to generate additional revenues. The overall cost recovery plan
was to adopt the principle that costs, normally borne by private
developers in private housing schemes, would be recovered through
the lease and sale of developed land, while those expenditures
normally ,incurred by government such as community facilities, would
be borne by the appropriate agency. On this basis, about 25%
of the project capital costs were to be recovered through local
revenues and consumer tariffs (off-site works, community facilities,
water and electricity) and were not to be included in the plot
charges.
5.03 The outcome did not meet Appraisal expectations since
P344.993 M or 50% of the overall project cost-of P689.125 M is prog-
grammed for recovery from the line agencies, the cities and the NHA.
The percentage for Dagat-Dagatan is even higher at 65% but the Regional
Cities component more closely approximated Appraisal estimates.
Although implied, but not quantified at the appraisal, the project
was expected to realize a substantial surplus principally through
commercial/industrial lot sales. On the basis of the latest estimates,
almost P435 M is projected as profit, principally from Dagat-Dagatan
(P425M). Both the residential lots and the commercial/industrial
lots are programmed to realize profits ofyPl]lM and P315 M respectively.
However in the regional cities, profits of only P9.00 M are expected but
considering the extent of the commercial/industrial development in
Davao and Cagayan de Oro, this amount should have been much higher.



69
5.04 WJhile Dagat-Dagatan is clearly expected to realize
substantial profit, the financial performance of the SIR component
is disappointing. For instance, to break evenon the basis of the
agreed cost allocation principles, P119.313 M would have to be
recovered from the sale of residential lots and a further P38.895 M
from the disposal of the commercial/industrail areas. From the sale
prices adopted only P76.951 M is to be recovered from the-residential
lots resulting in a deficit of P42.362 M (Cagayan de Oro P11.197 M;
Cebu 012.539 M and Davao P18.626 M). This deficit, however, is
planned to be offset in Davao and Cag4yan de Oro through the net
proceeds of the sales of commercial/industrial areas (estimated
to be P32.35 M in Cagayan de Oro and P19.05 M in Davao) and by the
NHA and the Cities (P4.30 M) from their own revenues. In Cebu
the loss will be made up entirely by the City since there are no'
comiercial areas for disposal.   All power and water costs, however,
are progranuned for recovery through user charges. Tables 5.1 and
5.2 summarize the costs allocated and those to be recovered according
to the major project components for each City. Annex II details
the figures at appraisal and actual.
5.05 Nevertheless, the disposal of comuercial/industrial
lots within the regional cities is well below that projected and
as of December 31, 1985,only 17,700 sq.m., hap been disposed. Some
.209,500 sq.m. or 92% of the completed remains undisposed. Although
prices were set at current market rates, most of the sales enacted
are through installment contracts from which only limited collections
(P1.38 M) have been made to date. The concept of using profits
generated from the sale of these lots clearly has still to materialize.
and although the cities and the'NHA do retain the assets, their li-
quification remains in the pipeline., Serious financial problems
are likely to occur unless these lots are disposed quickly and
revenues realized soonest. On this performance, there must be some
doubt about the actual demand for Such land in -these'areas; ade-
quate demand analyses were never undertaken and insufficient attention
has been given towards marketing. In Dagat-Dagatan efforts have
concentrated on the sale of industrial land with 78% of the completed
area disposed as of 31 December 1985. Although disposal is through
installment sales and profits will only be realized over the longer
term, total collections of P15.17 N exceed the total debit of
P11.88 M as of December 1985. The disposal of the commercial area
has been much sloe'rwith almost 296,000 sq.m. or 96% remaining
unawarded.  This is the direct result of policy decisions of NHA
to delay sales until -market conditions improve,inth6hope of realizing
higher revenue but at a later date.
5.06  Residential lot disposal, in the regional cities, too,
has lagged behind the completion of physical:works. As of December
31, 1985, approximately 36% of the 10,200 completed lots were
unawarded. More progress has been achieved in the sites and services

projects than in the slum upgrading sites. In New Matina (Davao)
and Kauswagan (Cagayan de Oro) sites and services projects 66%
and 75% of the completed lots have been awarded.    However in the
slum improvement projects, no awards had taken place iniLapasan
(Cagayan de Oro); only 18% of the lots in Pasil and Alaska (Cebu),
14% in Piapi and 3% in New Matina (Davao) and 31% in Macabalan
(Cagayan de Oro) have been disposed. Annex IV.
5.07 Collections from the residential beneficiaries and
those under the SBL and HML programs are low. As of December 31,
1985 total collection by the cities (Davao and Cebu) and NHA
(Cagayan de Oro) from the beneficiaries amounted to P6.14 Million
or 53% of-the total due with arrears standing at P5.74 Million.
Net collections for the fourth quarter of 1985 stood at 8% of the
total due in Cagayan de Oro (23% for the residential lots, 2%
for HML and 3% for SBL). Low collection rates for the same
period are evident on the residential lots in Cebu (less than 1%
of the total due) and on all SBL and HML accounts (less than 4%
of the total due). Residential lot collectionsin Davao, however,
were better at 28% of the total due.
5.08 At appraisal, it was assumed that funding for the SIR
program of the cities would be through loan, financing from NHA.
These loans would be repaid through project revenues which at that
time were estimated to be sufficient to service project debt and to
generate surpluses to finance future projects. Unfortunately, this
has not been the case. By the end of 1985, Cebu City although
virtually current in its payments to NHA were utilizing funds drawn
almost entirely from the City budget. In Cagayan, the local govern-
ment withdrew and NHA is collectinq directly from beneficiaries.
Payments from Davao and to a less extent Cebu have lagged and
total arrearages as of December 31, 1985 stood at P6.6 Million (Davao
P6.1 Million). Clearly the lack of attention paid to cost recovery
from the beneficiaries is manifested in Davao's attitude towards
payments to NHA and the City has refused to utilize its budget to
pay because of the alleged failure of NHA to complete land acquisi-
tion in a timely fashion which prevented sales.
5.09 In Dagat-Dagatan Phase ITI, 5,650 of the 8,349 resi-
dential lots h-ve been disposed as of 31 December 1985. Of these,
63 beneficiaries have paid in full. Total collections have reached
P12.21 M were P2.42 M as of December 31, 1985, more than 3 times the
monthly potential. Of the resi-commercial lots, 273 out of 1,517 were
disposed as of December 31, 1985. Collections from these accounts
have reached P2.71 M mainly through advance and down payments.
Clearly, there is still room for improvement regarding the regular
collect4ons but lot disposition on a large scale was only achieved
during the last quarter of 1985 on account of the late implementa-
tion of the main water supply by MWSS.
5.10 Cost recovery continues to be the weakest aspect of
shelter projects and, indeed, of all government projects involving
lending in the Philippines.  While problems specific to the Urban II



74
project are examined in detail in this report, it is important to
note that,no segment o-f the Philippine government is notably success-
ful in collecting dues from citizens.and that available sanctions are
seldom if ever.enforced. Numerous studies, point out that government
rarely collects more.than 50% of property taxes, business permit fees
are routinely undervalued by 200-300% and more large scale government
loan programs to small and medium business (Biyayang Dagat, KKK, etc.)
have recovered virtually nothing,of billions of pesos invested. In
short, government programs are widely viewed by politicians and
beneficiaries alike as hand'outs.
5.11  Cultural impediments-aside, there are.three major.
causes of low cost recovery:-
(i) the failure to dispose completed lots in a timely
fashion;
(ii)  the lack of "legal"'beneficiaries.from.whom to collect
particularly in the upgrading areis where many original
beneficiaries have transfered their rights for "illegal"
occupants who under presen-t regulations cannot legally
purchase the lots they occupy; and
(iii) the low sales volume in the commercial/industrial areas.
5.12 Much has been said and written about cost recovery but
little will be accomplished until the following fundamental steps
are taken:
(i)  title to land must be-acquired before sales commence;
(ii)  beneficiary selection..regulations.must be revised to
accommodate turnover;
(iii) sales documents must be executed much earlier in the
project cycle;
(iv) commercial/industrial sales must be' handled by competent.
professionals; and
(v)  sanctions must be enforced on beneficiaries whose accounts
fall into arrears.
B.      nd a   Affoabilit          -.
5.13 At appraisal, the proposed tenure-was for residential
beneficiaries to be offered a 25-year transferable lease with an



75
option to purchase the freehold interest. Subsequently, very few
lease options were entered into, since the beneficiaries preferred
and could afford to purchase on an installment basis. Initially,
Deeds of Sale with Mortgage were taken up, but these presented
their own problems. First, it was not legally possible to execute
a mortgage without title and land acquisition was still incomplete
in some project areas even after completion of construction. Second,
Philippine law requires a one year waiting period before a mortgagee
in default can be evicted. Thus, even when titles were available
the DSM was not efficient. In the end, a Conditional Contract to
Sell (CCS) was employed which did not require that title be clear
and in hand. Under this arrangement, beneficiaries only receive
the lot title upon full payment of all amounts due.
5.14 Final sales prices were higher than those estimated
at appraisal principally as a result of the increased land acqui-.
sition costs. In the regional cities, the average sale prices for
slum upgrading and sites and services lots in Cagayan de Oro projects
were computed at Pll,oU.0O0ZagainstP5,800.&P8,200 at appraisal, in
Cebu average prices of P4,900 against P3,100 at appraisal and in
Davao P5,400 for slum upgrading and P12,300 for sites against P2,600 and
p8,800 at appraisal. Nevertheless, affordability was not adversely
affected and the higher prices were offset by increasing family
incomes with benefits materializing much later than programmed
(Table 5.3).
5.15 In most slum upgrading and in New Matina sites and
services projects, within the regional cities, prices were set below
cost. Prior commitments, encouraged by the local government, to the
communities were made well in advance of knowing actual project
costs (particularly land) and these were impossible to retract.
However, in Kauswagan sites and services project (Cagayan de Oro)
prices were set without beneficiary or local government interference
resulting for once in a financially viable project. Nevertheless,
in all computations, neither interest during construction nor all
project administration costs were included.
5.16 Prices could have been somewhat higher in almost all
projects without exceeding theoretical affordability limits.
However, in the upgrading projects, beneficiaries were keenly aware
of the structures imposed by LOI 686 on cost allocation and, it was
agreed during negotiations with the Bank that prices for all projects
would be finalized and announced prior to the start of construction
at each site. This well-intentioned agreement actually resulted
in "premature" pricing of several early projects. When delays in
land acquisition forestalled initiation of cost recovery,it was
virtually impossible to amend agreed prices and interest which accrued
in the interim could not be directly recovered.



76
.17   In Dagat-Dagatan, two pricing schemes were adopted
for the ocial residential lots, one for some 1,500 censused house-
holds from Tondo under which subsidies amounting to P78.1 per square
meter were included, the other for the balance of lots disposed
-through  an open lottery., Here, the prices were set at market rates,
far higher than direct costs with "mark-ups" of between 50% and
130% depending upon lot size and locatiorv. This has not impaired
affordability since the smallest lot is afforded by households at
the 6th percentile of the mid-1985 urban income distribution in
Metro Manila, and the largest lot by households at the 44th percentile.
However, the prices are based upon costs which do not include water
supply nor sewerage.
C. Turnover
5.18 Under the SIR program, water supply, electricity,
community facilities and off site works were constructed by the con-
cerned agencies using their own funds. No turnover was therefore
needed. However, in Dagat-Dagatan, all works with the exception
of the inittal land fill were funded arid constructed by NHA. Following
the cost recovery approach adopted for Dagat-Dagatan wherein expenses
nomally incurred by Government Agencies in support of private
agencies, 65% of the total development cost for Phase III is allo-
cable to line agencies and local governments although paid for by
NHA.
5.19 The handover of roads and other public works to MPWH
has been significant with 100% of those in Phase III turned over
as of December 31, 1985. Five (5) Deeds of Transfer (DOT) affecting
Tondo Foreshore and Dagat-Dagatan projects have been signed in a
span of 5 months with the first signed in 5 March 1985 and the-last
in 18 July 1985. Total value of works turned over for operation and
maintenance amounts to P200.061 M for Phase III. However, no firm
agreements have been reached to reimburse the expenditures funded
through loans and NHA equity.
5.20 The turnover of water and sewer lines inclusive of the
SewageTreatment works to MWSS has not yet been effFted but nego-
tiations are progressing and agreements are almost on hand. The
total cost allocated to MWSS for Phase III is P83.63% or 28% of the
total cost chargeable to line agencies.
5.21 While the turnover of community facilities to the-local
government has progressed, there is little likelihood of their
reimbursement of the expenditures incurred. A loss to the project
is contemplated. Taken overall some P300 million for Dagat-Dagatan
Phase III remains to be reimbursed or assumed by the line agencies
and local governments. Unless this is achieved, the estimated profit
will be reduced substantially from P425 million to P126 million.



77
D.  Impact on Municipal Finance
5.22 The projects were designed to promote local economic
activity principally through the development of serviced commercial/
industrial land and were excepted to expand the local tax base.
It is likely that actual revenues realized are extremely low and
are not sufficient to cover the cost of routine services that the
cities and the municipalities normally perfonm. In reality, these
services are not being undertaken and without satisfactory main-
tenance, the improvements will soon deteriorate.
5.23 In Dagat-Dagatan, NHA is maintaining the completed
infrastructure using its own resources. In the regions, SIR projects
generally receive the same minimal maintenance that the local
governments provide throughout the country. Improvement should
be forthcoming in Davao and Cagayan as a result of RCDP/Urban IV
equipment acquisition and training activities. However, local
governments continue to budget scant resources for maintenance
almost entirely on a political basis with very little activity*
occuring unless elections are imninent.
5.24  Since collections from beneficiaries in the regional
cities are low, the local government will need to commit funds
from other revenue sources to repay NHA. All in all the improve-
ments expected to the City finances remain a long way off.
E. Replicability of the National SIR
5.25 To date, the program in the regional cities has not
proven its viability, since current revenues are not enough to
service the debt burden. No surplus has been built up such that
the second phase of the program in Davao and Cagayan de Oro (also
under a World Bank loan - the Regional Cities Development Project)
and in Cebu require a total infusion of new capital. The oppor-
tunities to generate capital from the profits of the sale of
commercial/industrial land has been abandoned since these revenues
are now needed to subsidize the loss resulting from the approved
sale prices of the residential lots.
5.26 Many financial benefits proposed under SIR program
have been lost because of the long delay in realizing revenues
resulting in the steady erosion of capital invested through low
pricing and poor collections. Much of the blame must be attributed
to institutional arrangements which placed an undue work load on
the local governments regarding cost recovery. Such politically
inspired agencies cannot under the present circumstances be expected
to effectively implement a just and equitable cost recovery program.
Changes are now proposed for future projects in the regions to reduce
the role of local governments in program implementation and to shift
the financial burden to other more appropriate agencies.
