79
5.27 On the other hand, in Dagat-Dagatan, initial computa-
tions show a replicable program, although, the policy of disposing
commercial/industrial lots, through installment   sales, means repli-
cability in the longer tenm and not as assumed at appraisal over the
short temn. However the lack of progress regarding the reimbursement
of expenditures made on behalf of other agencies is likely to reduce
profits significantly to the detriment of the much needed growth of
NHA capital.
E. Summary and Lessons
5.28 Pricing - the lack of consistency in the application
of a finm pricing olicy in the regional cities, often as a result
of pressure from the communities and local politicians, has jeopar-
dized the concept of full cost recovery. Too often prices were
established on the basis of estimates which did not include real
costs. In Dagat-Dagatan, on the other hind prices largely were set
at market rates without impairing affordability. This approach must
be applauded and shows the way forward for future projects.
5.29  Reimbursement - significant expenditures were incurred,
particularly in Dagat-Dagatan, on behalf of other agencies with
little or no attempt at the outset to ensure their recovery. There
are still no finm agreements for the reimbursement and/or the assump-
tion of liabilities by concerned agencies for these expenditures
amounting to P297 million.
Despite legal requirements and agreements, NHA continues to
experience difficulties in collecting payments from other agencies.
NHA had very little recourse in this instance other than to negotiate,
cajole, and wait. Future projects should require that specific
budgetary provisions be made: (1) from the national budget for com-
ponents allocated to MPWH, MWSS, and 2) as statutory items in local
government budgets providing a continuing basis for repayment of
laon obligations. Statutory appropriations in local budjets are an
established practice and should be clear requirements in any future
subsidiary loan agreements.
5.30 Profits - these have still to materialize to the
extent projected at appraisal as a result of the policy to sell lots
on credit and the deliys in disposing lots principally in the regional
cities. Policies regarding the development and marketing of commer-
ciallindustrial land must be improved. The slow rate of disposal
of these areas are clear indications of insufficient attention being
given towards market demand analysis and the lack of an aggressive
marketing strategy. Credit sales in Dagat-Dagatan and the suspension
of sales in the commercial areas, mean that revenues will only be
realized in the longer term. This must bring into question the
wisdom of incorporating large scale commercial/industrial develop-
ments in the projects. The reliance on profits expected to be



80
realized must be downgraded, unless it can be demonstrated that there
is a real demand and that profits can be realized quickly through
cash sales with credit sales the exception rather,than-the'rule.
5.31  Residential lot disposal - lot awarding has been slow
and documentation not efficient.   Admittedly, problems over land
titles has been a major factor, but no attempt was made earlier to
seek alternative tenure arrangements. Still some 54% of all resi-
dential lots remain unawarded overalls-(32% in Dagat-Dagatan and
72% in the SIR projects).
In future residential lot disposil, documentation and the
start of collections must be speeded up. Land should not be developed-
unless NHA has a clean title. Lot awards should closely follow or
even predate the completion of development and collections should
start immediately upon lot award.
5.32 Collections - no real attempt at collecting dues from
beneficiaries has been made in the regional cities.   People have
been allowed to remain on lots without paying, no serious effort
has been made to enforce'sanctions and starting dates for compulsory
collections have been continuously'put forward. The collection effort
in Dagat-Dagatan, has' suffered from the:Effects of strong community
pressure groups exerting political influence whenever NHA attempted
to enforce sanctions on beneficiaries and the operation of.a fully
computerized billing and coll.ection system has suffered numbrous
technical setbacks.  It will now prove to be a difficult task to
bring collections up to an acceptable level. -A vigorous enforcement
of sanctions on erring beneficiaries by NHA however remains in words,
and not deeds.  The Cities-show even less will to enforce collections
and it must be concluded that the.local governments are too politically
involved-to sustain a'continous collective drive. In the future
alternative arrangements must be explored.



VI. INSTITUTIONAL PERFORMANCE
A.   National Housing Authority
6.01 The project represented a large step forward for NHA
in terms of establishing its position as the country's dominant
housing institution. The national mandate provided by LOI's 555,
557, and 686 represented both an affirmation of NHA's new central
role in the country's urban development efforts as well asi,a major
challenge to the young institution's ability to move aggressively
beyond the confines of Metro Manila. The project also required
that NHA rapidly improve its capacity to manage the projects it
built. The Appraisal Report clearly noted that a major project
risk was continuing skepticism among some government officials
concerning the project's low development standards and uncertain
prospects for cost recovery (SAR, pg.44). No sooner was the
project underway in 1979 that the newly fonmed Ministry of Human
Settlements (MHS) attempted to merge NHA with its subsidiary
corporation involved in financing middle and upper income completed
units. NHA managed to emerge from this in tact, but competition
for funds increased and relations with MHS were strained at best.
6.02 The National Program - while the Dagat-Dagatan compo-
nent of the project accounted for more than two-thirds of total
cost, it was basically a technical challenge with limited signi-
ficance in terms of NHA!s overall institutional development. Of
greater importance was the effort to initiate a truly national low-
cost housing program through the Regional Cities Component. Prior
to the project NHA had very limited operations outside Manila, and
a new Regional Peoject Department (RPD) was created in 1979.
RPD's initial organization was very much oriented to the task of
assisting local governments in the technical aspects of-project
planning and construction.
6.03 The process of establishing working relations with local
government was delicate and based largely upon direct negotiations
between NHA's General Manager and the City Mayors. The project's
attempt to link local governments to a centralized national agency
was a marked innovation, effectively allowing NHA to harness local
resources while avoiding the overhead expenses of establishing
its own regional offices. In addition, the cities entered into
subsidiary loan agreements with NHA, assuming responsibility for
repayment to NHA of project investments at 10% interest over 25
years. In short, the project's attempt to establish a national
program outside Manila was heavily dependent upon the participation
of local governments.



82
6.04 By late 1979, all three reglonl cities had approved
subsidiary loan agreements with NHA and ambarkedl upen itiitial upgrading
projects. Progress was quite good until the local elections of
April 1980 which brought in new mayors in all three cities. Long delays
were encountered due to skepticism on the part of new city executives.
Local staff turnover as a result of patronage appointments was prevalent
and a major impediment. The City of Cagayan withdrew from the project
altogether and lIHA was forced to take over.
6.05 Meanwhile, NHA's attention shifted back to Manila were
Urban III/ZIP project preparation was well advanced. The initial
momentum in the Regional Cities was irrevocably lost and RPD was left
to cope as well as it could. RPD succeeded very well considering
its logistical problems and limited resources and the Regional Cities
component of the project was successfully constructed. However, NHA
did not succeed in forging the solid institutional linkages between
central and local governments so critical to the project's attempt
to establish a national program.
6.06 Organization - NIHA underwent two major reorganizations
during the project period, in 1978 and 1981. The main feature of
the 1981 reorganization was the creation of the Financial Services
Group with a much clearer delineation of basic functions. However,
little was accomplished with regard to decentralizing critical support
and post-implementation functions. While Manila projects can be
effectively serviced under the 1981 structure, the dispersed regional
projects still receive limited and sporadic support especially with
regard to legal problems, estate management and cost recovery, and
marketing of commercial/industrial areas. Table 6.1.
6.07 The problem of NHA's organizational bias towards Manila
will be exacerbated when the P400 M shelter component of Urban IV/
RCDP is completed in 1989. By that time, more than P600 M will have
been invested in six regional cities under Urban II and IV alone.
The rapid expansion of NHA's national program will soon outstrip the
limited resources devoted to the implementation of regional projects.
NHA's continuing role in the attempt to establish a national low-cost
shelter program will be decided in large part by the way in which it
meets the demands for decentralization and regional organization.
6.08 financial Performance - NHA's financial perfomance has
improve somewhat in recent years. A genuine awareness of and commit-
ment to cost recovery has emerged particularly as internally generated
corporate revenues have assumed an increasingly important role in
continuing operations. Still, as discussed in Section V of this
report, inadequate progress has been made in removing basic impedi-
ments to recovery of costs. At least five different departments
within NHA are involved in the process of acquiring and subdividing
titles, selecting beneficiaries, making awards and executing sales
documents, marketing C/I areas, and actually collecting payments in

any given project. Until this interrelated process is recognized
and dealt within a more unified manner, financial perfonmance will
continue to suffer.
B. Local Governments
6.09 As briefly discussed in the previous section, local
government played a major role in the project's Regional Cities
component. The project constituted a major change for local govern-
ments by requiring the following innovations:
a. Achievement, virtually overnight, of a political
consensus that relocation policies directed at
squatters for more than thirty years were wrong;
b. Acceptance, particularly in Davao and Cagayan, of
easily the largest single debt in the cities
histories;
c. Organization, virtually from scratch, of project
staffs requiring scarce technical skills capable
of dealing with a highly complex engineering and
administrative task, yet willing to work for lower
wages than prescribed by national government;
d. Expropriation of valuable properties in central
city locations from locally influential owners;
e. Acceptance of long-temn involvement into local
affairs by both national government and a variety
of foreigners;
f. Construction of the largest civil works projects
in the cities' histories entirely outside of the
established city engineering organization; and
g. Commitment of each city as a whole to a twenty-
five year debt on behalf of a little understood
low-income population, many of whom were already
technically guilty of breaking the law by squatting.
6.10 The enormity of the changes to be wrought in local govern-
ment were probably not appreciated at the project's outset. NHA was,
initially, not in a position to offer much assistance due to its own
extremely limited manpower. Expatriate advisors could assist on
technical aspects, but were not equipped nor employed to deal with
the complexities of creating new institutions withih the conservative,
insular local government systems.



85
6.11 It is surprising, therefore, that the regional cities
governments responded as well as they did to the project. As noted
above, all three cities had created project teams, entered into
project loan agreements, and begun initial construction by 1979, the
year of loan effectiveness. But the local elections led to new admi-
nistrations in all three cities.
6.12 In Cagayan, the new mayor had grave reservations about
the project loan agreement and, eventually, withdrew the city alto-
gether, While relations with the Cebu administration were congenial,
a fully staffed and equipped project team never completely jelled.
The city seemed always to look upon the project as-a politically
expedient but losing proposition, and loan payments to NHA are, to
date, financed almost entirely from the general city budget.
In Davao, a penmanent City Housing Office was created and it
functioned reasonably well. But a true political consensus in
support of the project never formed.
6.13 As a result, NHA was in 1985 seriously considering assuming
direct control of all regional cities projects under Urban II and IV.
Details of this plan were being worked out when the national adminis-
tration changed.
6.14  The fundamental issue remains, however, are local govern-
ments,the appropriate lead agencies for regional cities development
programs? If they are not, as some would argue, then two major insti-
tutional alternatives suggest themselves:
a. NHA could assume direct control of regional project
design, implementation, and administration as it
has done for all Metro Manila projects. It effect,
NHA would have to fully regionalize its operations
as have major ministries of government. This would
require a substantial reorganization of NHA's
highly centralized structure and a substantial
increase in operating costs. In the current Phil-
ippine environment it does not seem likely that
sufficient funds would be available for such an
expansion.
b. As advocated by the Mayors of several regional
cities semi-autonomous local housing authorities
similar to the;existing local water districts could
be established. Under this alternative, NHA would
directly administer Manila projects (as does MWSS)
while local housing authorities with their own
corporate identities would assume the functions now
assigned to local governments. The principle
advantages of autonomous local housing authorities
would be insulation from city political influences
and the ability to attract and-retain qualified



86
staff due to higher allowable salaries and benefits.
The principle constraint, as in the case of NHA
regionalization, is lack of funds.
6.15 Clearly, the expansion of shelter projects outside Metro-
Manila cannot continue until this major issue is resolved. The role
of local governments is the single most important variable in the
continuing effort to establish a truly national shelter program in
the Philippines.
C.  Interagency Coordination
6.19 As in N1rban I, the project suffered numerous difficul-
ties in coordination between NHA and, notably, the flinistry of PLblic
Works and Highways (MPWH) and the Metropolitan Water and Sewerage
System (MWSS).
6.20 In Dagat-Dagatan, the then Bureau of Public Works could
not fill the project site to.agreed levels. NHA was forced to
contract additional earthworks in excess of one million cubic meters
(in addition to removing large amounts of unsuitable fill materials)
the costs of which were unforeseen at Appraisal.  Likewise, the late
implementation of two major roadways, C-3, C-4, by MPWH resulted to
additional costs by NHA in the construction of lengthy access roads
leading to the different phases of the project.
6.21 However, MPWH has been cooperative with regard to accepting
almost P200 M worth of Dagat-Dagatan infrastructure for wliich it is
responsible by law for maintenance purposes.
6.22 MWSS upgraded their water and sewer specifications in
1981 affecting the implementation of awarded infrastructure contracts.
Modifications in the specifications initially escalated contract
costs and resulted in delays. This move by MWSS however, had its
positive effect over the-long term period in terms of a more effective
system and.relatively reduced maintenance costs.
6.23 After lengthy negotiations, the MWSS, by mid-1986, had
still to accept the physical transfer of the water and sewer systems
at Dagat-Dagatan for operation and maintenance purposes. Agreement
on the assumption of financial obligations also has still to be
finalized.
6.24 In the regional cities, other government infrastructure
agencies had much smaller roles in the project. MPWH has not ful-
filled its commitment* regarding community facilities in any of the
cities. This aspect of the project was-extensively studied during
preparation and appraisal ,but has now been more or less abandoned
to the whims of the MPWH budgeting system. Coordination with the



87
Ministry's regional offices has proved futile since all budgetary
matters are settled in Manila.
6.25  In summary, interagency involvement was not ideal.   In
future, NHA should 'not construct or finance any items which are the
responsibilities of other agencies unless funds are available feom
the appropriate budget, and this condition should be made a loan
covenant.
6.26 Attempts to coordinate with non-infrastructure agencies
were also largely futile. Government banks and small business
advisory agencies of the Ministry of Trade and Industry never became
involved in the business loan component as expected at Appraisal.
The Ministry of Social Services Development (MSSD) was helpful in
Dagat-Dagatan but not in the regional cities. UNICEF input to the
project amounted to little more than occasional seminars and acqui-
sition of some equipment. The experience with UNICEF was particu-
larly frustrating, and under RCDP/Urban IV the project Wams have
despaired of ever meeting the constantly changing UNICEF requirements
for project proposals.
D. Consultants
6.27  The project included a large technical assistance component
aimed primarily at improving construction supervision and fostering
the institutional development of NHA and local governments.
6.28 Construction supervision did improve and the general
quality of works under the project was acceptable. Numerous and
costly technical difficulties which arose at Dagat-Dagatan, however,
were unforeseen at Appraisal, calling into question the feasibility
study for that project which was prepared almost entirely by consultants
and upon which the Appraisal was largely based.
6.29 Attempts to strengthen NHA technically were notably
successful.. NHA's sharp distinction between Bank-assisted and in-house.
projects severely limited the extent to which broad changes could be
assisted by advisors whose inputs were limited to the Urban II projects.
6.30 Construction of the regional cities component were greatly
adied by resident planning and engineering advisors. Future advisory
inputs in the regions should place greater emphasis on the gridual
and difficult process of developing local capacity to manage
programs during and after implementation.



88
E. The Bank
6.31 Appraisal of the project was thorough although somewhat
optimistic and the groundwork was well laid for the following
Urban III and IV loans.
6.32 Several features of Bank performance throughout the
project period are noteworthy as lessons for the future:
a. Staff Turnover - membership of supervisory mission
after 1979 was unstable. As mission membership
changed emphasis on different project aspects also
varied causing a good deal of confusion among local
staff and advisors.
b. Technical Bias - during the crucial project imple-
mentation years (1980-84) mission supervision con-
centrated almost exclusively upon civil works
difficulties mainly in Dagat-Dagatan. Although
mission members regularly called for increased
emphasis on cost recovery, little substantive
advice was ever given until late in the project
which engendered a cynical attitude on the part
of local staff.
c.  Multiplicity of Functions - from 1981 onward the
regional. 'cities component went virtually unsuper-
vised by the Bank due mainly to competing responsi-
bilities in preparing Urban IV and the Central
Visayas projects. Learning from the problems of
ongoing projects was therefore minimized. Further,
the main Bank emphasis upon preparation of new
projects was reflected in the orientation of local
staff resulting in negligence of the ongoing
project. Local staff resources were stretched very
thin, and to some degree the Bank must accept
responsibility for the inevitable lack of attention
to Urban II which resulted.



89
VII. ECONOMIC RE-EVALUATION
7.01 The project was designed to have "a substantial influence
on the government's approach to urban shelter and infrastructure".
Significant equalization of access to basic public infrastructure in
key urban areas was targetto!d through the provision of services to low
income and squatter families and the regularization of tenure on the
basis of a substantially self liquidating project.
7.02 In order to justify the project economically a standard
cost-benefit evaluation was undertaken at appraisal. This exercise
has been rerun with the completion of project using a methodology
which follows the same principles adopted by the World Bank for the
Appraisal Report, and under which net project benefits are computed and
discounted to arrive at an economic rate of return for each component.
7.03 The measurement of net economic benefits for the project
is based upon the increases in property values brought about by the
provision of infrastructure and other services and through the develop-
ment of serviced, residential, commercial and industrial sites. Other
benefits which have certainly resulted, including direct and indirect
employment generated as a result of project implementation, improved
health and better education facilities and an improved environment
cannot be quantified directly. Increases in property values therefore
have been taken to represent a composite measure of the value of improve-
ments undertaken. In addition specific benefits in the form of room
rentals have been included for the house construction and improvement
which resulted from the implementation of the building loans program.
7.04 Land values were established from actual records of land
sales within or near to the project areas. They reflect market values
as of 1985 for residential, commercial and industrial lands in Dagat-
Dagatan and the Regional Cities. The market land values were converted
into annual rental values according to the prevailing economic terms
for rent. Room rentals were established from actual rates prevailing
within the project areas and reduced to allow for land rent already.
included in the assessment of land values. Project life was estimated
to be 25 years from the final completion of the project in 1985 although
the benefits for residentail land in the upgrading sites were assumed
to be realized in accordance with the disbursement-of civil works
expenditures, while the benefits of the commercial/industrial areas and
the residential lots in the sites and services projects were assumed
to match actual and projected disposal.
7.05 Project costs which represent those actually occuring
(or estimates where no firm date was available) were converted to
mid-1985 prices. Phasing of costs matched those expended with the
exception of land which were assumed during the first year of project
implementation. For all project sites within the regional cities, the
cost of land was its opportunity cost reflecting the best estimate of



real market value prior to development. In Dagat-Dagatan the oppor-
tunity cost of land was taken as the difference between the value of
unserviced land and the cost of fill and reclamation needed to trans-
form the site into developable land.
7.06 The financial costs for all civil works, design and
supervision and technical assistance were extrated from accounting
records' of actual expenditures, although estimates were made regarding
the cost of some components constructed by other agencies. These costs
were adjusted to economic costs as follows:
a. To 1985 prices using accepted price inflators;
b. estimated taxes were deducted;
c. shadow pricing was undertaken for foreign costs
(1.2) and unskilled labour (0.8) in accordance
with NEDA practice; and
d. additional costs were included for Dagat-Dagatan
to reflect land fill undertaken by the MPWH and
apportioned expenditures of facilities and works
constructed outside Dagat-Dagatan Phase III but
partially serving that area.
Maintenance and operating costs were established on the basis of a
formula relating to capital outlays, while the cost of collections
were based on estimates of collectibles.
7.07 The economic rates of return for the project and its
components compared to those at appraisal are shown in Table 7.1.
The rates of return are for Dagat-Dagatan below those of appraisal,
however, for the SIR program, with the exception of the slum improve-
ment component, they are near to or above the appraisal figures.
Nevertheless, the overall project return of 19% is below the expected
34% at appraisal, because:
a. additional costs items were uncurred, particularly
land reclamation and fill in Dagat-Dagatan;
b. the project took more time to complete: than originaliy
planned resulting in higher costs (principally as a result
of inflation) and a corresponding reductfon and delay in
the realization of benefits;
c.  the disposal of marketable land, particularly the commercial
and industrial areas, has been delayed; and
d. the benefits computed at appraisal (particularly land
values) were probably too optimistic.



91
7.08 As usual, this type of analysis does not capture very
significant additional project benefits including increased employment,
improved education, reduction of property losses due to fire and floods,
and improvements in health and general productivity. One study in the
main Davao upgrading area, for instance, showed that:
a. incidence of serious gastrointestinal diseases was
reduced from 60% to less than 10% of the project
population following implementation;
b. infant mortality rates, three times the national
average before upgrading, fell to slightly above
that average by 1984;
c. Only 9% of children aged 0-6 (61 out of a sample of
703) were suffering some degree of malnutrition
in 1984 versus more than 80% prior to the project.
VIII. Conclusions
A. Achievement of Objectives
8.01 The project succeeded to varying degrees in achieving its
stated objectives (SAR, pg. 8). Improvements in sanitation and basic
infrastructure were dramatic in upgrading areas and more than 11,000
new residential lots were developed through the sites and services
program.
8.02 Regularization of land tenure was less successful and sales
of more than 60% of residential lots are not complete. Delays in land
acquisition hindered the project and land costs increased dramatically
over Appraisal estimates.
8.03 Promotion of employment through commercial/industrial
development and small business loans was also less successful than
anticipated. A large percentage of commercial land remains unsold.
The small business loans program did not reach its intended scale due to
withdrawal of government banks but despite a variety of administrative
difficulties, a large number of new jobs were created in project areas
and NHA remains committed to the program.
8.04 Efforts to assist institutional development were notably
successful in the case of NHA, less so with regard to local governments.
NHA's financial management capacity improved greatly although actual
recovery of costs is still inadequate. Efforts to develop lasting
institutional capabilities-at the local government level were successful
in only one of the three project cities. The role of local governments
in future projects remains a major question mark with critical impli-
cations for the national shelter program.



92
8.05 In general, the basic approach of upgrading and serviced
sites development is now well accepted within the country and NHA has
demonstrated a substantial production capacity. However, further .
enhancement on the "soft-ware" aspects of project implementation and
management can be achieved by the experience learned from this program.
B. Loan Covenants
8.06 With several notible exceptions covenants of the loan were
generally fulfilled. Annex 5 summarizes compliance with covenants.
C. Lessons
8.07 A number of lessons should be drawn from the Second Urban
project for application in future undertakings:
a.  Decentralization and Regional Orenization- the expansion
of the national shelter program cannot reasonably proceed
until the role of local governments is determined. Full
regionalization of NHA's operations may simply be too
expensive given the current economic difficulties in the
country. If local governments are to continue as NHA's
development partners then much tighter on-lending terms
must be embodied in loan agreements, including statutory
city budget appropriations sufficient to service repayments
to NHA.
b.  Interagency Coordination - The interagency approach can
only be successful when the roles and scope of agency
participation are clearly defined and agreed upon through
formal covenants during project design. The aggressiveness
of NHA to perform and comply with its commitment to the
World Bank led to implementation of works normally under-
taken by other agencies, later proving to be detrimental to
its recovery program. NHA. should not in the future finance
projects in this manner, unless specific loan covenants have
been formally agreed and release of funds are through separate
budgetary releases.
Future projects should likewise consider execution
of works by other agencies themselves with assurances of
a timely delivery in the implementation of its component
in the context of the overall program.
c. Land Acquisition - the toal unreliability of judicial
expropriation as a method of acquiring land-has left no
recourse but negotiated purchase, as is now the NHA practice.
This method of acquisition is, however, also unsatisfactory
in that Government is subject to the capricious whims of
landowners in the negotiation process. An equitable and
simplified method by which Government can acquire land for
public use is urgently needed.



93
d. Commercial/Industrial Areas and Gross Subsidies - C/I area
de-velopment under future projects should not be simply
eliminated as the result of unsatisfactory experience in
Urban I and II. Rather, NHA must be encouraged to :
(1) improve market studies in the preparation phase to
avoid over supply; (2) employ competent professionals to
undertake sales and assist in establishing realistic pricing
structures; and (3) maximize integrated development as was
the case in the Urban II and IV overlap in the Davao C/I area.
e. Beneficiary Selection - A critical step in the cost recovery
process is beneficiary selection.. ,Current procedures in NIA
are too combersome. It is unreasonable to expectthat lOOX of
original beneficiaries in any project will remain there for-
ever. Turnover in both serviced site and upgrading areas
has been established at about 20% by NHA surveys (see Urban I
PCR Annex 1) and may run as high as 40% in some sites. A
formal procedure exists for NHA to approve a new occupant,.
but still is too cumbersome to keep pace with informal real
estate transactions in these areas. A realistic system must
be established by which "right" to lots may.be transferred
to new residents, and NHA's inability to police resales does
not obstruct its ability to complete the mortgage origination
document and thus begin collections.
f. Land Use - The utilization of reclaimed land forlow-cost
- sites and services projec,ts should be reviewed considering
Dagat-Dagatan's experience in incurring substantial cost
increases due to necessary works on soil stabilization. The
net economic benefit has been lower than anticipated in the
Appraisal due to this unforseen expenditure.
j.  Technical Assistance - The validity of over extended technical
assistance by expatriate consultants is raised from the point
of view of harnessing self-confidence and reliance on local
technocrats. The country should move towards encouraging
experienced professionals to form local consultancy groups
to programs/projects.
9. Project Acceptance - NHA should engage in a more agressive
information campaign prior.to project implementation to gain
wider acceptance to its programs from beneficiaries and local
government officials. A comprehensive knowledge of govern-
ment's honest effort and involvement to uplift their standard
of living should result in. better cooperation and confidence
in accepting their financial obligations accruing from the
housing benefits received by them.



94
h. Documentation and Lot Award - there should be flexibility and
foresight in adfressig theF problems of documentation and
award of lots pertinent to the situation at hand.
Due to the tedious process of expropriation which has
led to NHA's inability to secure and consolidate land titles
in some of its project-sites, a change in documentation was
only conceived in mi4-1984 leading to time lags in the
recording of sales reports since the effectivity of sales
was related to the signing of contract documents. The
reorientation period for the community to-accept this change
took some time and raised skepticism on the-honest intentions
of NHA.
i. Project Scope - Dagat-Dagatan is geographically well situated
wherein'the problems of housing can be addressed to, being
in the midst of a highly populated area and where housfing.
conditions were at their worst. Reclamation and the sub-
sequent additional filling have resulted in its beir, at a
most advantageous elevation when compared to the surrounding
low-lying flood-prone built-up-area.
Although the occurence of flooding in the regional area
has been found by MPWH as being a result of natural phenomena
(a marked increase in the sea and tide'levels) the unpopularity
of the project expressed by the nearby population has not
diminished on account of the disparity in the level of develop-
ment and attention of government between the project and the
surrounding areas.
Government should, in the future, consider development of-
a particular site, not in isolation, but together with-a
parallel program for development of the surrounding contiguous
area, where solutions to issues/problems in the region are
related to a wider field of application.
j.  Pricing - In most slum upgrading projects, initial lot prices
are presented to the communities in order to gauge public
acceptance to proposed developments. However, this approxi-
mation is perceived as final by the community and. difficul-
ties in collection have been experienced when-higher lot charges
are imposed based on actual'development costs. Prior commit-
ments by local government officials in this regard, have like-
wise pressured NHA to revert back to the predetermined cost.
In this respect, where pricing policies have been estab-
lished and have gained popular acceptanc'e, it is imperative.
to undertake cost control measures to peg final development
costs to committed charges.  Development standards should
be reassessed and mQdified during implement4tion whenever
necessary.
Cost recovery programs in low-cost fhousing can only
be successful if supported by the target beneficiaries.-

PROJECT COMPLETION REPORT
PHILIPPINES
MANILA URBAN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT (LOAN 12ZZT/1282-1-PH)
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Sectoral Context
1.01      The Metropolitan Manila Area (MMA) had an estimated population in
1975 of 5.5 million (132 of the national total), with an average annual growth
of S2. Projected at that rate of growth, greater Manila would have 23 million
people by the end of the century (302 of the Philippines' total population and
652 of its urban population). The reasons for locating the Bank's first
Philippines' urban project in the capitol city were compelling.  The MM had
severe deficiencies in shelter, water supply, health and nutrition, public
transport and institutional capabilities. Approximately half of the
substandard dwelling units in the Philippines were in the MEA, and this meant
about 1.8 million persons living on unserviced lots with inadequate sanitation
facilities and health services, compounded by flooding and stagnant water.
Incidences of typhoid, cholera and other gastro-intestinal infections and
malnutrition had been rising noticeably since 1968.
1.02      The MKA had a larger area (870 km2), a larger population base and a
faster annual population growth rate than other urban areas in the Philip-
pines. It also had a higher unemployment rate than the national average (112
in Manila compared to 7X nationally in 1971) and by far the worst housing con-
ditions in the country. While the proportion of the urban poor was about the
same in Manila as in other urban areas, Manila's poor was the largest concen-
trated poverty group in the Philippines.
1.03      Informal sector employment currently provides about 282 of Manila's
total - a relatively large proportion. One major reason for this is that from
1970 to 1980 real wages for both skilled and unskilled workers in Metro Manila
declined sharply to half their 1970 levels. Poverty would have therefore
increased, but families have supplemented formal earnings through increased
participation in the formal labor force as well as through private entrepre-
neurial activities. As a result, total household income actually rose (para
5.11) and evidence suggests poverty may have declined.
1.04      In transportation MMA was experiencing major problems.  Chronic
congestion had become a common condition because of population growth and
rapid growth in the number of private passenger vehicles.i In 1975, the HMA
had about 325,000, or 422 of a'l'l motor vehicles registered in the Philippines,
of which 70X were private passenger vehicles. The situation was exacerbated
by the inefficient use of existing road capacity. Public transportation was
mostly provided by the private sector, which had been reducing investments in
vehicle maintenance and fleet renewal because of deteriorating financial
conditions and uncertainty about Government policy.
1.05      Many of these problems were aggravated by the organization of local
governments in the MMA which was not conducive to effective metropolitan-wide
planning or administration. Municipal government was fragmented into 32
jurisdictions. However, in November 1975, the Metropolitan Manila Commission
(MMC) was established to control and coordinate 17 of these jurisdictions
including the four cities of Manila, Quezon City, Pasay and Caloocan.



104
1.06      The only previous Bank operation in an urban sub-sector (water) had
been a loan to the National Waterworks and Sewerage Authority (NAWASA) in 1964
to expand and improve the water supply system serving Manila. Project
execution was slow and the financial cc7epants were not observed. That
project was comploted after considerabl delays in 1972.    In the eorly 1970s,
with guidance from USAID, the Covernuez: made progress in organizing the water
sector, dissolving NAWASA and creating :he Metropolitan Waterworks and Sewer-
age System (HWSS), which was responsibl* for water supply and sewerage in the
Manila area, and the Local Water Utilities Administration (LWUA) for the
provincial cities. Four subsequent loans have been made for expanding
provincial water supply (Loans 1415 *nc 1710/Credit 920), and for Manila Water
Supply (Loan 1615) and Sewerage (Loan S19#).
S. Conditions Legsing to the Project
1.07      The largest slum in Metropoli:an Kanila and one of the largest and
most politically active in Asia was the Tondo Foreshore, the upgrading of
which was the centerpiece of this firsR urban project. It covered almost
180 ha and had a population of 180,000 persons (27,700 families in 17,500
structures). Median family income was about US$575 per year, about 602 of
median income in the KMA. Tondo's level of environmental sanitation was very
low because of the high population density, inadequate water and sewerage
facilities and poor drainage. Infant nortality was about 130 per 1,000 live
births per year, 802 of pre-school chi:dren were undernourished, and 301 of
the adult female population suffered from anemia. In 1974, there were about
500 hospital admissions for cholera in Tondo and 707 deaths attributed to
tuberculosis. Covernment's response t: the problem of slum and squatter areas
had been a series of ad hoc projects generally involving relocation to distant
areas with few employment opportunities and inadequate services, especially
transportation which was beyond the fi.ancial capabilities of the relocated
population.  The inherent defects of this approach led to the conception of
in-situ upgrading as an alternative/conplement to resettlement.
C. Principal Report Sources
1.08      The principal documents used in preparation of this report, other
than supervision reports and general c:rrespondence, are summarized in
Annex A.
II. PROJECT IDEMTIFICATION, PREPARATION, AND APPRAISAL
A. Origin and Background
2.01      The project was first mentic:ed in the Dank's files in August 1972,
and in October/November of that year az urban sector mission visited Manila.
Project identification involved the re-iew of a long list of major infra-
structure projects. The Tondo Foreshc?q upgrading was selected as the point
of concentration because of its size a:d critical importance and also to
demonstrate an alternative to covernme:t's expensive and ineffective slum



105
relocation policies.  The 1974 aerial photo survey of the Tondo Foreshore
served as the basis for a feasibility study carried out by a Covernment task
force on housing and urbsn development established under the Office of the
President with technical personnel from several agencies. In July 1974, a
socio-economic and physical survey was undertaken which detailed the squalid
conditions of Tondo. By October, the Tondo Foreshore/Dagat Dagatan
Development Authority (TFDDA) was established under Presidential Decree (PD)
No. 570 to implemnt a comprehensive development plan for Tondo, including a
nearby self-sustaining resettlement site (Dagat-Dagatan). With the formal
creation of the National Housing Authority,(NHA) in 1975 to consolidate the
various offices and six agencies which were then responsibl* for housing and
resettlement issues, the TFDDA was absorbed into the newly organised agency
and was renamed Tondo Foreshore and Dagat-Dagatan Development Office (TFDDO).
2.02      The ensuing Project was the first effort at comprehensive slum
upgrading and sites and services by the Philippine Government and the first
upgrading project of the newly created NHA. It was also one of the first such
projects financed by the Bank. The upgrading approach included both in-situ
improvement and resettlement of families displaced by project works as well as
other public works noted below in para. 2.03. The relocatign site was Dagat-
Dagatan, a 400 ha site of reclaimed fishpondsA three kilometers away from the
upgrading area. Because this was an entirely new approach for Government, as
well as for the Bank, trial and error helped to identify areas of weakness
elaborated on in other sections of this report. Arrangements were made for
MWSS to provide assistance in improving water supply and sewerage for Tondo
Foreshore and Dagat-Dagatan. In executing these infrastructure works amidst
very high density and a politically sensitive population which was also very
skeptical, the NHA was aware of the delicate nature of its community develop-
ment work and took steps to ensure that residents of Tondo Foreshore, played a
reasonable role in planning and approving important details of the work par-
ticularly with regard to the realignment of streets, utilities and houses - an
exercise referred to as "reblocking" (see para 3.06(d)).
2.03      Considerable development was taking place in 1975/76 in the area
immediately adjoining Tondo Foreshore. As a result, resettlement needs were
high. The International Port to the southwest was being expanded by the
Philippine Port Authority (PPA) with German (KFW) assistance. The first phase
of port improvements required the urgent relocation of approximately 1,300
squatter families. Some 2,500 more families qould have to be relocated to
clear the entire International Port complex. I In addition, reclamation and
construction of a fisheries port at nearby Navotas was nearing completion and
would require the relocation of another several thousand squatters. It had
therefore become the responsibility of PPA to find suitable accommodation for
nearly 7,000 families affected by all these works. An arrangement was made
with the NHA to resettle at least the first 1,500 households from the
International Port expansion into the Dagat-Dagatan Phase I site, as well as
/1 Figures represent appraisal estimates.



106
*nother 700 *ffected by construction of the R-10 and C-2 roads, for which DPH
was responsible which were ultimately accommodated in the KFr-financed Phase
II area. Mfurther S00 families were expected to be displaced from Tondo
upgrading,- (about 3x), briefing the total households to be resettled under
this project to about 2,700,- or about 40X of the total required. The rest
were to be accommodated by the KWF's Phase II.
B. Preparation and Appraisal
2.04      Project preparation was carried out by the various Government
agencies - primarily EHA with assistance from MWSS, the Department of Highways
(DPH), and the Department of Public Works, Transportation and Communications
(DPWTC). Assistance was also rendered by various short-term consultants and
supplemented by guidance from frequent Bank ition.    The initial GOP devel-
opment plan in December 1974 would have reiicstpl 702 of the families in Tondo
to distant areas and would have required high Levels of subsidy.  After a
review by a Bank mission in February 1975, a eoapletely different approach was
taken. This involved minimal relocation and then only to a nearby site in
Dagat-Dagatan where low-cost sites and services were to be provided. More
appropriate levels of service and cost recovery were proposed. These were
reviewed with community groups who welcomed the new approach, particularly by
ZOTO and Ugnayan, two of the more politically active groups. The project was
appraised in July/August 1975 and negotiations took place in March 1976.
C. Project Objectives
2.05      The project was fundamentally a demonstration to establish the
viability of a new and alternative approach to demolition based on in-situ
upgrading and serviced sites. This was clearly the central objective for the
Bank. For NHA, upgrading the quality of life for Tondo residents was the
primary objective. Though slightly different, these objectives were
compatible. Both required addressing the Tondo's basic shelter, sanitation,
health, employment and infrastructure deficiencies. In addition, the urban
transport and institutional problems in Metro Manila described in paras. 1.01-
1.07 were to be dealt with. The project was highly ambitious, outlining
objectives in five distinct areas:
(a) Upgrading/Serviced Sites: To demonstrate that upgrading in situ was
an appropriate alternative to resettlement, and to provide squatters
with security of tenure and adequate urban services in such a way
that beneficiaries could pay for the full cost of on-site services
and some portion of the value of the land.
2/   About 2,000 lots were to be generated in Dagat-Dagatan and 1,000 infill
lots in Tondo, so that resettlement committments fully absorbed new site
development.
3/   p. (vii) of the SAR.



107
With respect to serviced sites, to provide a lover unit cost
solution for new housing by means of serviced lots with full cost
recovery;
(b) Traffic: To focus on low-cost, high-return investments in urban
transport with urgently needed traffic control measures and
equipment for the efficient use of existing infrastructure;
(c) Health: To integrate comprehensive health, nutrition and other
social services with physical improvements in water supply, sewerage
and drainage;
(d) Employment: To experiment with employment creation by means of
supervised credit programs; and
(e) Institutions: To strengthen two new institutions, the National
Housing Authority (NHA) and the Metropolitan Manila Commission
(PMC), by providing technical assistance and in particular to
increase significantly NHA's capacity to plan and implement similar
slum upgrading projects in other parts of the Philippines.
D. Project Description
2.06      The Project was to include:
(a) Tondo Foreshore Upgrading. iUpgrading a 180 ha area, with an expec-
ted pop9lation of about 180,000 persons living in 17,500 struc-
tures,!' by providing basic urban infrastructure and health,
education and other welfare services, materials for house improve-
ment and cottage industry loans, about 1,OOO new serviced lots and
core units on now vacant land, and about 15 ha of land for indus-
trial and commercial use;
(b) Dagat-Dagatan. Development of a 40 ha residential community near
Tondo for some 20,000 persons by providing basic infrastructure and
health, education and other services, materials for house
improvement and cottage industry loans. About 2,000 residential
lots would be developed with simple sanitary core units for lease
with options to purchase by families displaced from the Tondo
Foreshore, and about 3.5 ha of land for industrial/commercial use.
Offsite infrastructure related to the two preceeding components was
also included. This principally involved (a) rehabilitation of the
4/ The area was later reduced to 137 ha. Expected population after
improvement was 160,000 persons and 15,000 structures. This implies
demolition of 2,500 structures as opposed to the 500 units referenced in
other sections of the SAR (see para. 2.03).



108
existing Tondo Sewerage Pumping Station, (b) construction of a new
outfall sewer to replace the existing outfall, which had deterio-
rated to the point of being inadequate for the efficient and sani-
tary discharge of sewage flows from the Tondo Foreshore. and (e)
construction of stabilization ponds and temporary (above ground)
water mains at Dagat-Dagatan;
(c) Major Roads and Bridges.   Construction of 5.4 km of the R-10 road
between C-i and Dagat-Dagatan, construction of 1.7 km of circumfer-
ential roads C-2 and C-3 and construction of 0.4 km of an access
road to the Dagat-Dagatan resettlement site and two bridges related
to these works. This access road would'also facilitate traffic
movement out of the Internatioqmal Port Area;
(d) Traffic Improvement. Construction of junction improvements, road
markings and footpaths, bus shelters, city-wide signals, signal-
linking equipment and mobile radios to improve the use of existing
infrastructure; and
(e) Technical Assistance. Consultants (about 46 man-years) to be
retained for ten distinct tasks involving:
o    detailed design/engineering and coAstruction supervision of the
physical works in Tondo and Dagat-Dagatan by NHA and MWSS, for
major roads/bridges by DPH, and for the DPI traffic component;
o    organization, management and financial planning for the NRA;
o    feasibility studies to develop a second urban project;
o    a Metropolitan Manila transport/land-use study by the DPWTC;
o    studies and training to be carried out by DPWTC on behalf of
MMC involving:
-    a fiscal base study;
-    a cadastral survey for Metropolitan Manila to update the
existing survey which covered only half the area;
-    a municipal services study; and
-    design and implementation of an MNC staff training
program;
o    a national transportation investment plan for 1977-80 and a
national tranSport systems study by DPWTC; and
o    strengthening the planning capabilities of the various
Government transportation agencies.



109
III. MAJOR RESULTS
Tondo Foreshore Upgrading
3.01      The project successfully introduced an alternative to Government's
socially disruptive and expensive policy of wholesale slum clearance, demoli-
tion and wholesale relocation to distant resettlement areas. In situ slum
upgrading has been accepted and expanded through subsequent Bank and inter-
nally financed projects. In the early 80's fully 415 Areas for Priority
Development (APDs) in Metro Manila alone were identified for in-situ improve-
ment. The EHA's Ninth Anniversary Performance Review (1984) indicates it has
upgraded over 44,000 structures in slum comunities of Metro-Manila, Cagayan
de Oro, Cebu and Davao since 1975, of which Tondo represented only 13,000 or
about a third. Production peaked at over 9,000 units per year in 1980-1982.
3.02      Resettlement, however, ha3 not halted.   Simultaneously over 63,000
families have been relocated for a variety of reasons or 40X more than
received in-situ upgrading. The NHA indicates all resettlement constitutes
legitimate removal from dangerous esteros, embankments and immediate develop-
ment sites for Government-funded projects. Questions have been raised how-
ever, about how much may have been motivated by aesthetic or other purposes.
Thus, overt demolition appears to have been, replaced by a blend of both in
situ upgrading and continued resettlement.
3.03      Perhaps the most striking achievement of the Tondo project is the
successful demonstration of the viability of self-help and progressive
improvement. Secure tenure and provision of basic services did, in fact,
elicit significant private investment and dramatic improvements in existing
housing. Before and after photos of Tondo (see photographs) show the remark-
able transformation of the communities' physical appearance over a ten-year
period from a virtual slum to a middle income neighborhood. Project
improvements did not, however, seem to drive out the poor. Shanties and very
modest structures still stand beside elaborate and obviously expensive
homes. Predictably a proportion of original residents have cashed in their
windfall profits and sold out. Turnover, however, appears to be somewhat
lower than in other slums in Metro Manila (20X vs. 27%) with about a third of
those moving out also registering property sales. The extensive amount of
mixed development would seem to reinforce the conclusion that turnover has not
been excessive. Owners of improved houses frequently sublet, which can
enhance household income to the extent that it more than defrays mortgage
costs. Though costs including markups virtually tripled, incomes rose by
about the same amount from an estimated median of P435 in Tondo at project
completion, to P1,200. Over 90% of residents could therefore afford a minimum
30 m lot.
3.04      The success of the Tondo slum upgrading program validated some of
the basic assumptions of the Bank's approach to slum problems, specifically
that security of tenure and adequate infrastructure are much more important
responsibilities of Government than housing construction itself. When these

113
are provided, housing tends to follow as a private investment. In Tondo more
than 90Z of the structures were improved to some degree, of which 122 were
completely new houses.
3.05      The upgraded infrastructure in Tondo dramatically improved the
environment and health standards. This was, to IHA, a more prominent objec-
tive than the policy changes which attracted Bank support. Low areas of
standing water have been filled, homes enjoy individual sanitation facilities,
walkways and streets make the area far more accessible, and schools, clinics,
Barangay centers, markets, and livelihood (job) centers are readily avail-
able. Crisp before and after health statistics are not readily available, and
if they were it would be difficult to establish causality between the project
and any demonstrated improvements. Nonetheless, environmental and health
conditions have visibly improved as a result of the project in the vast majo-
rity of the area.
3.06      Community participation seems to have been relatively successful.
This was due in large measure to the politically active character of the Tondo
population and the pivotal role of the barangay (neighborhood) as a socio-
political unit. It was also attributable to the special concern NHA devoted
to Tondo, although that approach has been criticized by some as being too
paternalistic. Despite frequent tension and conflict, barangay leaders turned
out to be effective partners in NHA's efforts to establish good working rela-
tionships with Tondo residents. They proved less reliable allies, however, in
cost recovery. Some have used the onset of maintenance problems as a politi-
cal wedge to actively undermine collection effort.
3.07      The NHA has had great difficulty extracting itself from maintenapce
responsibilities in Tondo. General inadequacies in service maintenance levels
throughout the city have led the community to attempt to hold the Authority's
collections hostage to better service from line agencies, ministries and
municipalities. This remains an issue on which follow-up under subsequent
PCRs will be needed. Even at the time of writing several physical problems
still persist in selected segments of Tondo:
(a) water supply is still inadequate in sections of Tondo affecting the
functioning of sanitary cores, although 90X of house connections
have been made. Residents in these areas must still purchase water
in containers;
(b) the drainage system is much improved over pre 1975 levels. However,
serious flooding problems are occuring in this area as well as other
parts of Metro Manila as a result of a combination of factors;
tc) solid waste collection continues to be a major problem, as it is in
much of the rest of Metropolitan Manila.
Dagat-Dagatan Serviced Sites
3.08      From a physical and social perspective, Dagat-Dagatan, like Tondo,
was a striking success. Families rapidly progressed over a few years from
temporary shanties erected on site to substantial homes (see photos).   Comer-



114
cial areas sprung to life and a vibrant community has evolved. This first
effort at serviced sites clearly demonstrated the viability of the self-help
principal as well as its acceptability to a low-income population.
3.09      Dagat-Dagatan successfully introduced new low cost, self-help
alternatives into Government's housing programs. Affordable serviced sites
with expandable sanitary cores were a new concept and a viable alternative to
overdesigned, subsidized multistory concepts as an affordable method of
addressing the shelter needs of low income families. These serviced sites and
upgrading programs are now incorporated into the 1983-1987 National Shelter
Program, though they represent only about 17X of the units. These are the
only programs which have proven afforable to the lower two-thirds of the
population. This remains a disappointment and it is hoped that subsequent
plans will begin to better address the match between supply and demand. Bank
sector work has focussed on this topic.
3.10      The first phase of Dagat-Dagatan was a distinct disappointment from
the standpoint of cost recovery. Though physically completed and occupied in
1978/79, collections had still not begun by the end of 1984, due largely to
delays in obtaining clear land title. The NHA had promised residents collec-
tions would not begin until tenure was issued. Expropriation of the land and
related court settlements dragged the process out for six years. Disputes
over the charging of core units exacerbated these problems. Only in early
1984 was clear title to the land obtained by NBA, five years after residents
had occupied the site. Though advance payments had been solicited, few were
received (8% in 1982 and 5X in 1983). Though collections were expected to be
difficult in view of these events, recent figures show considerable
improvement.
Major Roads and Bridges
3.11      Though still under construction at the time of writing, this
component is expected to be completed by the end of 1985 and to have substan-
tively fulfilled its objectives. Costs were well in excess of those expected
and, in retrospect, preparation work should have been further advanced on this
component before appraisal. Nonetheless, the economic rate of return is
expected to be very close to that anticipated.
Traffic
3.12      The traffic component is considered highly successful.    Physical
facilities were built within a reasonable time frame and achieved the results
expected including a high economic rate of return. The creation and develop-
ment of a respected Traffic Engineering and Management (TEAM) unit within
Government, that could effectively demonstrate the value of traffic management
within the time frame of a single project, was a major accomplishment. The
traffic management component has convincingly established the merits and
replicability of traffic management to Government officials. The TEAM has
been funded with a second tranche of investments (with OECF assistance) which
will effectively double the number of modernized traffic signal systems in
Manila. Based on the success of traffic management measures in Manila,
traffic components have been included in the Regional Cites Development



115
Project (RCDP-Loan 2257-PH) and the Municipal Development Project (PREMIUMED-
Loan 2435-PH). Another traffic management unit has been established in Metro
Cebu, and a proposed Bank loan (Central VisJyas Regional Project II) would
further the development of this unit.
3.13      The TEAM unit is still a separate project office not fully
integrated into Government, which exacerbates problems with interagency coord-
ination, budgeting, and staffing. Staff turnover remains high. Government
acknowledges these lingering problems and is beginning to address them. The
Bank is assisting in conjunction with the prepavation of the proposed Metro
Manila Urban Transportation Project which will follow up on these longer-term
institutional issues.
Technical Assistance
3.14      The project's large technical assistance component (46 man years)
included seven studies and attempted to address the major problems confronting
Metropolitan Manila. Studies were carried out on Metro Manila's transport
services and land use; and on the MMC's fiscal base.   A cadastral survey was
conducted to complete an earlier survey which covered only half the
Metropolitan Manila area, and a National Transport Investment Plan prepared by
DPWTC. Two additional studies led to the preparation and subsequent implemen-
tation of projects for upgrading (MMINUTE) and for upgrading and serviced site
development in regional cities. The final study amalgamated the records and
data of the several agencies which were merged to form the NHA. Much of the
technical assistance was directed to strengthening project implementation.
The MMC staff training program did not materialize except for a number of ad-
hoc short courses. Annex C summarizes completed studies and the Borrower's
view of their usefulness.
Livelihood
3.15      In addition to the five major project components, a small sum of
US$700,000 (1% of total project costs) was earmarked at appraisal for NHA to
experiment with small business ("livelihood") loans to create employment and
increase incomes among the target population. This component had mixed
results. The DBP and PNB extended loans totalling only about 35% of the
initial allocation and were ineffective in collections. As of June 1983,
average collection rates were just over 55% with PNB loans performing much
better (73%) than DBP (53%). In early 1^84 NHA moved to take over the port-
folio from the initiating Banks, and has been extending similar loans from its
own funds. These loans appear to have effectively reached the very low income
groups and do seem to have stimulated some incremental economic activity.
However, they are still at an early stage in establishing any degree of finan-
cial viability, and results to date are disappointing. The NHA's records show
still lower collection levels than DBP and PNB, but are complicated by an
artificial assignment of payments received to the mortgage first, and business
loan last. Since many borrowers have taken mortgages, and home material loans
as well as business loans, accurate data on collections is not available. The
NHA acknowledges that the livelihood loans are not presently financially
viable, but that it is functioning under government directives to make such
loans. Questions have been raised as to whether these activities disperse
NHA's attention and ought to be discontinued to enable the Authority to better
focus on its mainstream functions. These questions will be addressed under



116
the PCRs on Urban  I and III, both of which continued and expanded the liveli-
hood effort, as well as under preparation activities for subsequent loans.
IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION AND COST
A. Start-up
4.01      Start-up was delayed to about the same degree as most of the Bank's
first generation urban projects by a combination of institutional, technical
and political factors. Reasons for delay included squatter relocation and
contracting problems, due largely to the inherently difficult nature of the
task, and to the lack of experience among both IHA and Bank staff. This had
been flagged at appraisal as an expected risk. The project was based entirely
on preliminary designs which contributed to its slow start. Survey work began
on schedule, but the difficult nature of the site and the inexperience of
surveyors resulted in errors. These were corrected and the surveyors
replaced. This, in turn, delayed site planning and contract tendering.
4.02      Tondo's political volatility required careful community consultation
and relations - a task vastly underestimated at appraisal. Misunderstandings
which arose after appraisal and continued through 1976 regarding the nature
and objectives of the project required extensive community education by NHA
before project implementation could proceed. The sheer size and socio-poli-
tical complexity of the Tondo undertaking was unprecedented, and the Authority
virtually felt its way through this initial period, unaided by any truly
experienced guidance from either its consultants or the Bank.
B. Implementation Schedule
4.03      The project required seven years to substantial completion of
physical works rather than the four years envisioned at appraisal. Slippage
was thus about 75Z (Figure 1). The roads and bridges component lagged
substantially behind the overall project, requiring nine years to complete.
From actual start-up of this component, completion is expected to take six
years including design work. While project performance was significantly
behind appraisal estimates, it should be borne in mind that the original
schedule lacked the seasoning of actual experience with this type of project,
and was therefore rather unrealistic.
4.04      Roads and Bridges:  Delay in the roads and bridges component was
largely attributable to its slow start. Construction did not begin until mid-
1981 - the original project completion date. Loan extensions were therefore
twice granted to December 1983 and then December 1985. The reasons for delay
were (a) the lack of detailed design which was done following appraisal, (b)
delays in clearing squatters from the proposed road routes, which obstructed
survey work and design preparation, (c) the need to rebid the works due to
changes in Government's procurement rules at the time of award, and (d) the
decision to build a dual carriage-way which required substantial additional
counterpart funds.



117
13~~~~~~:
atI1  _InI;
- I  1 '  I;  _it    I  I 
-    111jJfjff 1111111' 1



4.05      Dagat-Daistan also got off to a late start, but was largely
physically complete by mid 1979 - about two years behind schedule. Civil
works took longer than expected becouse of low and varied soil bearing capaci-
ties, unanticipated subsurface conditions and obstructions, as well as NOl's
inexperience. "Software" features, however - principally the materials loans,
physical relocation and consolidation of new homes - were the principal cause
of delay. These tasks were expected to take 18 months but actually required
nearly four years. Such "human" factors were significantly underestimated and
explain much of the discrepancy between appraisal estimates and actual
performance.
4.06      In the case of Tondo Foreshore, delays were attributable to a number
of factors. First and foremost was the sheer magnitude and complexity of this
first upgrading effort, in combination with its experimental nature noted in
the preceeding section on project start-up. Many elements of the work were
simply not foreseen - or not adequately allowed for in the schedule. The
sequencing of work and orchestration of clearance and resettlement in such a
large area were not well understood. Thus the schedule itself was not a fully
suitable standard against which to measure performance. Seven other factors
proved major causes of delay:
(a) Survey Work: substantial errors and discrepancies were discovered
in the survey work carried out by contractors of NHA. These
involved missing structures, incorrect distances and errors in
elevations. Benchmarks for horizontal and vertical controls were
never tied in to one another and errors were generally so
significant as to render the exercise useless. The survey was
repeated, but while the quality improved somewhat, similar problems
arose and the second survey contract also had to be terminated.
Only in early 1978 - two years after Board approval, was a competent
survey team commissioned which satifactorily completed the work.
However, just as work by the third firm was getting underway, a
catastrophic fire swept Area 2 of Tondo, destroying 2,000 of the
Tondo's 17,000 structures, or about 12x. The physical, social and
political turmoil which followed slowed survey completion.
Throughout, survey monuments were lost or removed and workers
harrassed. Some were killed in a local brawl. Detailed design work
could not be started until the survey was complete. Inexperience of
local firms with this type of complicated survey in densely
populated zones obviously contributed to these problems.
(b) Subsoil Conditions and Obstructions: Tondo was physically an
exceptionally difficult area in which to work. As in Dagat-Dagatan,
Tondo was affected by poor soil conditions involving extreme differ-
ential settlements and saline groundwater levels less than a meter
below the surface, further complicated by extensive pockets of
rubbish, vegetation and scrap metal. Although soil boring samples
collected during preparation indicated that poor conditions could be
expected, actual conditions were far worse than anticipated. The
southern portion of Tondo was found to be underlaid by a maze of
uncharted buried utilities of such age and condition that breakage
and flooding of excavations seriously hampered work. Dense criss-



119
crossing in some areas of overhead telephone and electric lines
obstructed crone movements and restricted work clearances throughout
the very densely populated area and presented problems throughout
construction.
(c) Resettlement: Road and other rights-of-way had not been fully
cleared when construction started. This was particularly true where
only a portion of a dwelling obstructed work and had to be removed
by the owner who was inclined to procrastinate as long as possible
in the hope it might ultimately prove not necessary. Where entire
structures had to be demolished, residents resisted moving since the
Dagat-Dagatan site was not yet ready for occupancy. Work began in
late 1976, but the resettlement site was not ready for even prelim-
inary occupancy until 1978. Inadequate lead time had been allowed
for site development. Construction of major infrastructure com-
menced long in advance of "reblocking", and as a result, nearly all
work had to proceed in narrow, cramped lanes often little wider than
the excavation itself. A particularly vexing problem was the
constant danger of undermining foundations of structures in close
proximity to the work. The combination in Tondo of extremely poor
soil conditions, flat terrain and the absence of drainage rendered
work nearly impossible during the 3-4 month rains. This had not
been adequately anticipated.
(d)  Reblocking:  Regularization of plot layouts, referred to as
n-re-blocking", was envisioned at appraisal and began fairly early in
the project. It involved in-situ relocation of structures within
each block. Three alternatives were presented to the community
involving a range between quite modest adjustments to the existing
layout to fairly dramatic repositioning of nearly all dwellings.
After extensive discussion, the community opted for the most
regularized pattern involving tremendous disruption, effort and
expense. The extent to which this represented their own free choice
or an in-built bias of the planners themselves is not entirely
clear. The result was an amazing rejuggling of almost the entire
community. "Before "and "after" photos are shown in Chapter III.
Buildings were literally picked up and moved by community teams.
Others were demolished and rebuilt. The cost was estimated at P
2,000 per unit, which if applied to 80Z of the 14,000 eventual
dwellings added an unrecognized P22 million to the project cost, or
about a third of the expected unit development cost in Tondo.
Realization that these costs had to be fully covered by individual
families further slowed execution of this monumental undertaking.
Construction continued throughout the reblocking period. While some
of the reblocking followed road construction, civil works were
complicated and slowed by the exercise. (Under Urban IV (RCDP)
reblocking costs have been recognized and added to total project
costs. This expense is shared equally by all families as part of
the site development cost.)
(e) Work Quality and Contract Administration: Contract administration
was seriously deficient, especially at the beginning. Work quality



120
was poor and the pace of construction slow. Some works failed and
had to be repeated. Originally work was bid as one huge contract,
involving five major subcontractors. This gargantuan contract was
eveatully terminated for poor quality and general non-performance.
Work was broken up into smaller packages. Problems with quality
dogged civil works throughout project implementation.
(f) Community intervention: As noted under start-up difficulties, con-
siderable confusion existed in the initial 1974-76 period regarding
the project's nature and objectives. An intensive community educa-
tion campaign to dispel these concerns proved to be an ongoing
necessity throughout much of the project period. Even afte;. con-
struction began, all field personnel - regardless of their employer
(Em or contractor) or position   - were subjected to queries and
discussions with the community about the project generally and spe-
cific works immediately underway. Disruptions to residents' daily
activities were inevitable. Major conflicts between contractors,
NIA field staff and residents were surprisingly few under the cir-
cumstances, but instances of individuals intervening in construction
and confronting contractors with intensive grilling about the proj-
ect were widespread. Civil disorder, particularly violence among
youthful rival gangs in the vicinity of work in progress, occasion-
ally forced workmen to seek refuge from overt warfare. Resistance
from residents of one neighborhood resulted in a time consuming
court action also slowed work. Despite the extensive involvement of
comnunity leaders and groups, dealing with 180,000 individuals
proved extremely time-consuming.
(g) Coordination: The inter-dependence of various components of the
project demanded close sequencing, strict adherance to tight
schedules and constant coordination among implementing units and
line agencies. In the early years in particular, when agencies were
concentrating on their own area of responsibility with little
concern for its impact on the others, this coordination was
deficient and led to much delay. The establishment of an Inter-
agency Coordinating Comittee helped but did not resolve the problem
which remained a difficult feature throughout implementation.
(h) Materials Shortages: Scarcity of PVC pipes and cement following the
1978-79 petroleum price hikes also slowed work and resulted in the
loss of much of one building season.
C. Costs
4.07      Total project costs (Annex 8) were estimated at appraisal at P487.8
millio: ($65.0) based on preliminary engineering. Actual costs were P973.7
milliot or approximately US$91.8 million. This represents an overrun of 100%
in pescs and 41% in dollars. The differences between appraisal and final
estima:es to completion for the main categories of expenditure are as follows:
