4.08      The main cost increases occurred with respect to major roads and
bridges (2311), the sewer outfall (227%) and Tondo upgrading (1002). In
addition to the Bank and Borrower's general inexperience in estimating costs on
this type of undertaking, major reasons for the differences between appraisal
estimates and actual costs were:
(a) for Tondo, delays in start-up (para. 3.01), underestimated
quantities for land fill and reclamation work, where a variance of
more than 6001 occurred due to unforeseen subsurface conditions and
obstructions, hikes in land costs of 1502, and the generally higher
labor and materials costs resulting from the major 1978/79 petroleum
price increases;
(b) for the roads and bridges, major delays in start-up as well as the
price increases noted above. Designs here were preliminary and
should have been further along for reclaimed land such as this where
uncertainties can be high; and
(c) for the sewer outfall, four years delay in start-up, increased scope
of work for the interceptor and watermain across the C-2 highway, as
well as the cost increases noted in (a).
4.09      Savings were realized in the traffic component principally due to
the omission of the civil works element. Equipment costs only modestly
exceeded those expected (by 16%).



122
D. Disbursements
4.10      Disbursements on the original loan required nine years to complete -
with the project's profile very similar to the overall urban profile
(Annex C). Host first generation projects suffered similar start-up difficul-
ties because of the inexperience of both the Borrower and the Bank in these
types of operations, as well as the inherent difficulties of any demonstration
project which attempts major policy reform. Though start-up was quite slow,
depressing disbursements throughout the initial five years, momentum gathered
in the sixth and seventh years, when fully 402 of the total loan was drawn
down. If NHA were able to roll over its program on a continuing basis, this
would imply a drawdown capacity for a program type of loan of this size of
about five years. Disbursements followed a predictable "S" curve, though in
these early years, Bank staff were using a rather unrealistic straight line
method for appraisal projections (see Figure 2).
V. FINANCIAL ASPECTS
A. Development Costs
5.01      The pricing structure described in the Staff Appraisal Report was
modelled on the "private developer" formula, under which costs normally allo-
cated to homeowners in a private subdivisioxi were to be borne by benefici-
aries, and off-site infrastructure, community facilities (except barangay
centers), studies and technical assistance were to be borne by Government.
Off-site water mains and stabilization ponds were partially allocated to
residents since they would also serve wider populations. One unified direct
charge to beneficiaries for lots is shown in the appraisal covering land,
reclamation, surveys, drainage, water, sewerage, electricity, core units and
barangay centers. The NlA's understanding, however, was that a split pricing
structure would be implemented with MWSS and MERALCO recovering associated
utility costs through normal tariff charges. This would differ from the priv-
ate developers' model under which buyers pay directly for all utility infra-
structure. Beneficiaries were also expected to pay for NHA's administration
and interest during construction. Based on this formula, gross development
costs (total costs net of community facilities, technical assiE -nce, home
material loans and small business loans) were expected to avera6 . 168 per m2
o saleable land. In the event, actual development costs were about P 620 per
m or an almost fourfold peso inj;ease. In dollars, this was roughly triple
that expected (US$58 vs. US$22).)' While land and construction costs doubled,
much of this increase can be attributed to the longer implementation period.
5/   Using an average exchange rate of 10.6 over the life of the project.
Appraisal estimate includes all contingencies.

Appraisal estimate includes all contingencies in 1979 whereas actual
completion occurred in 1982.  The SAl estimate of interest during construction
also incorrectly assumed a one-year impleuentation period, artificially lover-
ing the estimated cost. For approximate couparison, appraisal estimates
should be increased by another 452 representing three additional years infla-
tion, and an appropriate four-year construction period reflected.   ThIse two
adjustments would bring the anticipated devglopment cost from US$22/a to
US$37/r' on about two thirds of the US$58/a . Annex J and Table V-1 outline
development costs and units costs per square meter expected at appraisal and
actual.
B. Sale Prices/Affordability
5.02      Sale Prices.  Subsantial cross-subsidies were to be applied to
residential lots from the profits from commercial and industrial land (about
10 of the total area) which were expected to realize about P 100 (US$13.3)
million, and from payments to NHA by PPA and DPH for resettlement of squatters
obstructing their development projects (pare 2.03). Anticipated profits
totalled P 118 million. Under the single integrated charge outlined in the
appraisal report the average sale price for rfsidential lots was expected to
be P 52 per m or P 2,600 for an average 50 m lot. Actual costs were over
ten times this figure - and would have resulted in a sales price of P 577 per
m or P 28,850 per average 50 m lot.
5.03      Predictably actual costs rose and profits as well as saleable land
shrank. As noted above, cost increases were attributable to capital costs
which doubled from P 187 million to P 406 million and (b) administration and
interest during construction which far exceeded expectations due to delays,
adding 502 to development costs.   Profits on commercial/industrial areas were
about a third of that expeg;edq and payments from line agencies (PPA and DPH)
never materialized at all.- Finally, saleable land - which was frequently
overestimated in first generation projects - shrank by about 201 (Table
V-1). The combination of these factors drove up actual prices significantly
above appraisal estimates.
5.04      Cross-subsidies from comercial/industrial units were a disappoint-
ment.  In theory, these differential pricing arrangements were rational,
creative, and seemed achievable. In practice they proved less effective. The
Philippines' deteriorating economy left NBA with a huge demand for low-cost
subsidized residential units and a much reduced comercial/industrial
demand. Economic shifts led to deteriorating market prices, and uncertainty
as to whether to pre-sell lots (running the risk of underestimating final
6/   The appraisal report makes no mention of the Vitas area which figured
prominently in NHA's own calculations of commerical profits under the
project. The NHA has contended that the Vitas Industrial Area is an
integral part of the Tondo project, and that profits would be used for
cross-subsidy of the residential area. See Annex M for a fuller
discussion of the Vitas property.



126
costs and selling at a loss) or waiting until completion (creating inventory
problems and downside risks of weakening demand and lowered prices). As noted
above, payments expected from other Government agencies did not materialize,
and saleable area for commercial/industrial fell significantly short of
appraisal expectations by about 30X. Sales lagged. As of December 1984,
coummercial/industrial sales stood at about 57X of the total. Assuming sales
are consumated on completed lots, the P 118 million expected profit at
appraisal will drop to P 37 million, or about one third. The Authority is
seriously rethinking the practicability of cross-subsidies and contemplating a
policy change under which sale prices on all units would fully cover costs,
and com erciallindustrial units would garner whatever profit the market would
bear. Economic downturns would then leave NHA less exposed.
5.05      The P 577 appropriate sale price at project completion was not,
however, the price actually charged beneficiaries. Three events altered the
pricing formula presented in the SAR. First, the pricing formula reverted to
a split charge, with utility infrastructure costs recovered through tariffs.
The appraisal report anticipates one unified direct charge to beneficiaries
covering nearly all infrastructure, core units, and land costs. In the event,
all water and sewerage (off-site, on-site and on-plot up to the house) were
charged to HWSS to be recovered through normal utility charges, and all elec-
trical infrastructure was built by MERALCO and recovered in a similar
manner. Assuming these agencies remain financially healthy, these costs will
still be recovered from beneficiaries, through utility charges. Thus two
charges emerged, one for lot mortgages and one (or more) for utilities.
5.06      The shift to a split pricing structure (direct charge and utility
charge(s)) did not alter the basic cost allocation formula (Annex K).
Families still paid directly for land, reclamation, survey, local roads and
footpaths, drainage, core units, barangay centers, landscaping, commercial/
industrial buildings and design/supervision. They paid indirectly (through
utility tariffs) for water, sewerage and electical infrastructure. The net
effect was to cut costs allocable to the mortgage by about half, shifting the
burden of collection on this sum to MWSS and MERALCO. As noted above, NHA
indicated at completion that it had always understood a split price would be
applied and did not consider this a change in project design. The unified
price may have been a presentational simplification by the appraisal team.
5.07      The appropriate sale price on mortgages based on the split pricing
structure (net of utilitiesI and after applicatiog of cross-subsidies should
have been about P 300 per m   or P 15,000 per 592 n  average residential lot
(Annex J). The actual price was P 95.29 per m about a third of that
required.  At the mandated price, a 50m   lot would cost P 4,760.   This price,
implemented under PD1314 issued March 20, 1978, represented the second major
alteration of the pricing structure presented in the appraisal report. It was
far more significant than the first.
5.08      PD1314 materially eroded cost recovery.   The Bank concurred in this
decision, however, and even encouraged it because three years had passed since
project start-up and the commuity was anxious to know what price they were to
pay for their lots. By setting a price, the NHA established the understanding
that costs were to be recovered. The Authority was confident the allowances



127
for price escalation were sufficient, and that costs would not rise further, a
judgement in which the Bank also concurred. Events proved otherwise. Actual
completion occurred only four years later following significant yrther price
escalation, triggered in part by the 1978/79 oil price increase.-
5.09      Finally, no markups were ultimately charged on the P 95.20 capital
recovery factor established under PD1314. The 6% administrative fee and 12%
p.a. interest during construction was subsidized by NHl.
8/
5.10      Affordability.  The premature mandatings-  of the sale price in Tondo
under PD1314 damged the project's cost recovery but further improved its
already excellent affordability. At appraisal, a lot in Tordo or Dagat
Dagatan (both priced the same) was expected to be affordabLe to about 85X of
the residents, assuming families could pay 182 of household income for project
services. Using the same 182 assumption, by 1982 at the time of physical comr-
pletion in Tondi, the charges that should have resulted from the   lit pricing
formula (P300 m ) were affordable to over 95X of Tondo residents - a distinct
improvement over the appraisal estimate. Table V-2 summarizes sales prices
and affordability at appraisal and at project completion.

5.11      Despite cost increases and protracted delay, it appears the project
would have met and even exceeded its affordability target of 852 based on
actual costs. The imposed subsidy under PD1314 was unnecessary. The reason
for this is simply that while development costs including markups tripled
(Table V-1) - so did incomes. Tondo's median income rose from the 435
projected for 1978 at expected completion to 1200 at actual completion in 1982
- a nearly threefold increase. Over 902 of Tondo residegts could afford the
P95 (US$9) appropriate monthly charge for a minimum 30 m lot. The further
reduction to P30 ($3) under PD1314 achieved little in terms of greater afford-
ability to residents, but placed a singificant financial burden on the NHA
totalling about P80 (US$7.5) million.

C. Cost Allocation
5.12      The major impact of the three pricing adjustments was to
dramatically reduce direct lot (mortgage) charges (from 822 to 37X of total
upgrading/serviced site costs) and double subsidy levels (from 182 to 35Z).
About 28Z of costs were passed on to the utility companies which are still
expected to recover these costs from beneficiaries. In total, beneficiaries
should have paid 80X of upgrading/serviced site costs and actually paid 65Z.
The NHA picked up the difference with its share of costs rising from nothing
at appraisal to 17Z, or P 88 (US$8) million. Table V-3 presents the cost
allocation anticipated at appraisal and actual (see Annex K for details).

D. Tenure, Sales and Collections
5.13      Tenure.  At appraisal the anticipated form of tenure was a leasehold
with option to purchase. The two advantages of this form of tenure were (a)
the ability to issue the lease against a writ of possession, before land
acquisition was complete, and (b) ease of eviction in the event of non-
payment. However, because the Tondo community had for years been promised
title by Government, political pressures rapidly mounted to :hift to deed of
sale (title) with mortgage (DSM). NHA eventually promised the community
individual land title. However, expropriation procedures resulted in
protracted land acquisition and at the time of writing, NHA possessed title to
only two thirds of the area. Some form of contract was required to initiate
collections, and in 1984 the Authority decided to revert to the CCS for
properties as yet untitled. Though this decision met with some resistance
from elements within the community, it represented the only practical
alternative.
5.14      Sales.  Partly because of land acquisition problems, as well as
other factors, sales (including mortgage documentation in upgraded areas)
lagged. At the time of writing three quarters of residential lots and only
57Z of commercial industrial units were sold/documented (Table V-4). Of the
remaining residential lots in Tondo, 28Z were affected by duplicate titling



130
*nd legal complexities, unresolved census cases, resales and encroachmaents.
Since virtually all new serviced sites were utilized for resettlement,
selection of beneficiaries did not feature in this first project. The
inability to clear squatters from commercial lots in both areas - including
one large contractor - also stalled operations. Heavy price escalation of 50-
601 in 1984 as well as lower than expected prices led to a temporary
suspension of marketing. However, activity was resumed in early 1985. Table
V-4 presents the December 1984 status of sales.
Table V-4: SALES

5.15      Collections.  Cost recovery has been singularly the weakest aspect
of project implementation. Start-up of collections was significantly delayed
among resettled families occupying Dagat-Dagatan, where occupancy occurred in
1978/79 and collections began only in January 1985, due to lack of title as
well as to a longstanding dispute regarding the charging of core units.
Compulsory collections began in Tondo Foreshore in mid-1973, about a year
after infrastructure completion (see Chart 27277). Though well behind SAR
estimates this was not unreasonably behind service delivery. Thus NEA's
project collection experience spans only about 18 months and reflects only the
initial stages of this phase of the project.
5.16      To date, the Authority has collected about 56Z of the amount due
(612 in Tondo and 491 in Dagat-Dagatan). Advance payments and full payoffs by
families with relatives employed abroad initially masked mounting arrears.
For example, for 1984 which was effectively the first full year of serious
collection effort, P22.19 million was due and actual collections were P27.34
for Tondo and Dagat-Dagatan - fully 1231 of that expected. Arrears, however,
rather than declining, mounted sharply during the first half of the year,
prior to rescheduling.
5.17      The low level of collections is partially attributable to the slow
start-up of initial collections in Tondo, where there was no previous history
of payment. The considerable sociological difficulty of attempting to
establish a habit of regular monthly payment among a large squatter population
accustomed to free occupancy must be acknowledged and constitutes a second
major factor. Third, more thorough preparation and advance work and greater
lead time would have done much to strengthen the critical start-up period. A
fourth factor has been that until recently, the NHA showed little real will to
collect. With the Bank and Government supplying the majority of its
investment needs, other pressing - usually engineering - matters took
priority. The Authority's perspective on this last factor differs from the
Bank's. They attribute delays in collection start-up principally to NEA's
verbal committment to the community that payment would begin only upon full



131
delivery of tenure and services. This comittment was made early in project
implementation to gain the confidence and cooperation of Tondo residents.
While tenure vas issued to many at earlier stages, services were not fully
functional before late 1983, water pressures as noted herein, are still
inadequate in several areas of Tondo, road maintenance is poor and serious
periodic flooding recurs. Doubtless these comunity commitments comprise a
fifth and important cause of low collections. Establishing a distinction in
the co munity's mind between "delivery" of a service (NHA's responsibility)
and its "maintenance" (the job of local govex-nment and utilities) has been
difficult. When maintenance is poor, people stop paying.
5.18      The Philippines' 1983/84 economic and financial crisis created new
incentives for cost recovery. Government funds were less forthcoming, and
Bank projects and thus disbursements slowed. As debt service mounted on NHA's
previous borrowings, cash grew short. Local borrowings became prohibitively
expensive. For these and other reasons, a major 1984 drive was mounted to
improve corporate collections.
5.19      The year brought three major changes.   Management information on
collections was sharpened and focussed on six basic indices and the scope of
regular reporting extended to all NHA's project areas, producing a complete
picture of collections for the first time. In June, PD1923 was issued, giving
beneficiaries a three month period in which to pay off arrears and/or
reschedule their payments. Penalties on delinquent payments were forgiven for
this period if delinquent accounts were paid in full or rescheduling arranged
by September 30, 1984, though no write-off of principal or interest was
granted. By September, P2 million of the P4 million total arrears in Tondo
had been paid in cash. Rescheduling gave both beneficiaries and NHA a clean
start. Finally, NHA issued public notice of its intent to foreclose on
seriously delinquent cases, though it has never before enforced cases of
repossession and resale on low-income lots since its 1975 inception.
5.20      The 1984 drive produced results.   Collection on the Bank's total
portfolio with ttO/Authority nearly tripled from P13 million in 1983 to P37
million in 1984,     A main drive also occurred to establish new accounts in
non-Bank sites, with 26,000 accounts added to the roster. Total collections
rose from P65 million in 1983 to P101 million in 1984 - a 55X increase. It
remains to be seen whether these measures will result in sustained improvement
in collection efficiency. Serious obstacles still exist in the farm of
mounting political opposition to tightened collections during an economic
downturn, and in the legal one-year redemption period during which NHA may not
resposses or sell off a unit. As a result the unit therefore remains a non-
performing asset for about two years. Subsequent PCRs on Urban II and III
will follow up on this aspect.
10/ New accounts increased by only 14X so do not explain the increase.



132
E. Infrastructure Turnover and Maintenance
5.21      Turnover of completed works to MWSS, MEIALCO and local governments
has proven a major headache. Completions have been significantly delayed,
with attendant cost increases. Despite an earlier Memorandum of Agreement in
principle (with MWSS only), negotiations over final asset values and
construction quality have led to protracted delays of up to five years for
some works. Meanwhile NRA must maintain completed infrastructure.
5.22      Of the P234 million total in completed works for handover, about 602
is attributable to MISS and 402 to MPWH. To date, the MWSS has accepted about
582 and the KPWH 1OOZ of these works under Deeds of Transfer (DOTs). Overall,
turnover stands at 76X. The DOTs formally transfer assets and responsibility
for payment of any commensurate IBRD debt. They also acknowledge the equity
investment of NHA as a debt. So far, however, no payment method or schedule
for repaying NHA equity has been set. The NHA plans to tackle this problem
after agreement has been reached on the total amount of the obligations under
Urban I. Alternately NHA may have to adjust paid-in capital for these
amounts. The City of Manila accepted all of its minor roads in January 1985.
5.23      Maintenance has been problematic.   Delays in turnover left NHA with
the formal responsibility and expense of maintenance. In addition, the
unavoidable immediate use of services (e.g. roads) in an occupied site like
Tondo results in the immediate onset of deterioration. As noted in para 4.17
inadequate maintenance and dysfunctional services (e.g. low water pressures)
have contributed to low payment levels. Hopefully the progress made in 1984
in turning over works will help to separate the concept of service maintenance
from mortgage payments responsibility in the community's mind. This will take
time, however, as well as an active community education campaign which was
launched in the spring of 1985. The NEDA has suggested means be explored in
future projects for involving the community itself in maintenance activities.
VI* INSTITUTIONAL PERFORMANCE AND DEVELOPMENT
A. National Housing Authority (NHA)
6.01      The NHA's performance was strong in some respects and weak in
others. It should be borne in mind when assessing institutional performance
that this first project was essentially a physical demonstration of an
unprecedented approach - in situ upgrading and self-help housing - in a huge,
complez, and politically volatile community. Just building the physical works
represented a major challenge. Community relations introduced complexities
and NHA did much learning along the way, adjusting its approach as it



133
proceeded. The project's institutional objectivesUl. did not go such beyond
building NHA's production capacity - which was achieved, and a viable cost
recovery performance - which was not as of the date of this report. The
Authority has also had difficulty in handing over infrastructure and extracing
itself from maintenance responsibilities in completed sites. Intensive
efforts are evident by MHA to improve its performance in this regard, and the
PCR on the second and third Projects will reflect the results.
Product Delivery
6.02      The expansion of NHA's production capacity for low cost upgrading
and serviced sites represented a principal project objective and one of its
primary achievements. The Housing Authority at project inception had
virtually no track record in this type of operation. Through this first
project it has upgraded about 14,000 dwellings (vs. 15,000 estimated at
appraisal) and resettled another 2,500 (vs. 3,000 estimated), fundamentally
affecting the lives of roughly 180,000 people.
6.03      The Housing Authority is the only agency producing housing
affordable to the lowest two thirds of the population. The majority of its
production is targeted on lower income households through serviced sites,
upgrading and resettlement, though it also produces some completed housing,
condominiums and rental apartments. It is presently reassessing the merits of
its completed housing production program, and considering withdrawal.
6.04      As of 1981, five years after project start up, the NHA reported
annual production of over 18,000 units. Production slipped in 1982 to about
16,000 due in part to the deteriorating economy and in part to the normal
lumpiness of annual figur T17 This represented about half of total production
by the Government system.-
Financial Performance
6.05      The Authority's financial performance has significantly lagged its
physical performance.  Engineering and construction concerns dominated the
agency's attention for the first five years. Staff and management alike were
immersed in the extensive difficulties of such a major physical undertaking.
Insufficient attention and particularly lead time was devoted to "software"
project features such as land acquisition, titling, and cost recovery. At a
broader level, the NHA's management of its financial affairs was similarly
11/ Other objectives such as the projects demonstration effects are covered
elsewhere in this report (see paras. 2.05, 3.01, 6.02 and 6.04).
12/ Including the shelter finance system of mortgage purchases by National
Home Mortgage Finance Corporation (NHMFC). Source: Public Investment
Review - Shelter Sector, December 1984.



134
weak.13/ There was little evidence that the Authority intended - or desired
to establish itself as a financially viable housing corporation independent of
continuing government subsidy/equity contributions. In most respects it
behaved more like a government department, relying on direct government
infusions to supplement Bank loans, and devoting little serious effort to
establishing or improving its corporate receipts (collections) or internal
operating efficiency. Government, in turn, periodically directed the
Authority to perform tasks which it might not, of itself, have elected to
carry out. In this respect the Authority has not differed dramatically from
many - if not most - other publicly held corporations in the Philippines.
Centralized directives and laxity in corporate financial performance have been
pervasive problems and are only now being addressed by Government.
6.06      The Authority has recently begun to focus attention on improving its
financial position. In addition to the 1984 cost recovery drive, a new
pricing policy is under consideration which would begin to stem the leakage of
its capital through subsidies, and it has recently initiated moves to sell a
portion of its mortgage portfolio in order to revolve its capital. These are
all positive signs.
Institutional Maturation
6.07      Three distinct phases seem discernable in the NHA's gradual
maturation. In the early years of project design and start-up, social and
political factors featured heavily in the agency's operations. Housing was a
politically sensitive topic. As a result, policy change was difficult.
Upgrading and self-help site and service programs were poorly understood by
both government generally and the Tondo community in particular. The NHA
itself had little technical capacity in these areas - a factor acknowledged by
the appraisal report. The project's second phase which extended throughout
most of the execution period was therefore devoted predominantly to building
its technical and production capacity. Consultants employed to do detailed
design and supervision helped, but much of this learning came from trial and
error by the staff itself. As the agency gradually improved its grasp of the
physical side of its operation, attention began shifting to the financial
aspects of its business. As noted above, cost recovery and collections
performance have now become the principal focus of attention. The Philippine
financial and economic crisis of 1983/84 probably sharpened NHA's attention to
these matters by virtue of extensive cutbacks in Covernment funding in
combination with the growing debt service on Bank loans.
6.08      No doubt this process has taken far too long.   Fully a decade has
passed since project appraisal, and cost recovery performance is still not at
a satisfactory level. There is reason, however, to expect measurable improve-
ment under the third project, which focuses on broader aspects of NHA's
institutional performance. Just as it took NHA five years to build reasonably
13/ NHA indicates that while attention to and management of collections was
weak to begin with, they feel other aspects of their financial management
were satisfactory.



135
solid technical capacity, it will take several years of concentrated attention
to establish a satisfactory collections performance, and probably several more
to sharpen its financial management.
6.09      While some housing agencies in other countries have traversed these
stages more rapidly than the NHA, it is difficult to judge in the Philippine
context what a reasonable time frame might have been. The appraisal
expectation of four years was overly optimistic; the reality of a decade or
more probably too long. To judge institutional performance the project must
be placed in context.
Environment
6.10      All project agencies operated in a difficult environment,
particularly in the latter years of project implementation. Politically,
economically and administratively, the Filipino environment presents major
obstructions to effective performance. The politically sensitive nature of
housing and slum upgrading accentuates many of these problems for the NHA.
Local inflation over the project period was estimated at 10% per year for 3.5
years of active construction and in the event averaged 12.6Z over six years
(1977-82), skyrocketing in 1983 and 1984 to unprecedented levels of up to
50%. The four devaluations of the 1983/84 period only marginally affected
construction costs, but it, tandem with inflation severely eroded family
affordability just as cost recovery was getting off the ground. The heavy
1984 inflation created serious political problems with collections in Tondo.
Budget cuts, disruptions in flows of Government funds, very low civil service
salaries and highly politicized decisionmaking all make it extremely difficult
for Filipino institutions to perform well, both operationally and
financially. In addition, NHA serves a market segment that wields little
economic clout and constitutes a relatively powerless if vocal minority of a
highly stratified society. The creation of the Human Settlements Development
Corporation (HSDC) and its subsidiary Bliss Development Corporation (BDC)
under the MHS system initiated competitive activity and a merger attempt in
the late 1970s. As economic conditions deteriorated, competition for funds
became fierce. In this environment, the NHA's physical accomplishments are
indeed commendable, and their poor cost recovery performance though not
acceptable, is at least more understandable. A comparison of NHA's corporate
collections performance vis-a-vis other Philippine corporations as well as
other housing agencies in the region would better place performance in context
with other agencies of a similar nature and age, but is regrettably beyond the
scope of the present PCR.
Otganization/Staffing
6.11      The Authority's original organizational structure (Annex D) was
altered only slightly during implementation. The Relocation and Resettlement
Department took on the new upgrading function and a separate Commercial/
Industrial Estate Department was established by 1978 when the second project
was appraised. However, a major reorganization occurred in 1981 under which
the Metro Manila Projects Department (MHPD) was strengthened, and the
Financial Department was split into units focused on technical functions and a
unit to handle overall financial management.



136
6.12      Buildup of professional staff levels was a major achievement of the
first project. The Authority tripled in size from 1,100 staff in 1976 to a
peak of 2,900 in 1983. Staff turnover presented problems throughout implemen-
tation, but does not appear to be a significantly greater problem in NHA than
elsewhere. The Authority is seriously concerned about manpower development
and runs a number of well conceiTed programs for its managerial, professional
and support staff. Strength in :echnical skills still outweighs financial
capacities. However, the Author.ty has built a staff capability to keep the
environment under surveillance t3 do policy analysis, to think, plan, program,
monitor and evaluate. Such capa:ities exist only on paper in many institu-
tions. While ample scope exists for improvement, these functions at least
appear to be real and alive in Y3A.
Reporting
6.13      Information systems an! reporting were initially weak, particularly
on civil works and project costs and cost recovery but improved substantially
over the years. Financial information on actual collections remained poor
until 1984 when significant impr3vement occurred. In the ensuing year a
thorough monitoring system was established for NHA's entire portfolio - a
significant initial step towards improved collection performance. Financial
management indices are still no: closely monitored by eithe14 jhe Bank or NHA
and accounting practices are de-ficient in certain respects.   'Rectifying
both should constitute a principal focus of future loans.
Coordination and External Relations
6.14      The project required extensive coordination between Government
Ministries and agencies, a task wihich was assigned to NHA in addition to its
responsibilities as executing agency for the Tondo Foreshore and Dagat-Dagatan
components. This involved primarily six entities including the DPH - which
executed the major roads component, MWSS - which executed the sewage outfall
station, DPWTC for the traffic management component, and DBP/PNB for adminis-
tering the line of credit for cc:tage industry. Schools were built by NHA on
behalf of the Ministry of Education and health centers for the Ministry of
Health.  Other community facilities such as playgrounds and barangay centers
were also built for the community, a seventh and most complex entity with
which NHA required close liaiso:.
6.15      As forewarned in the appraisal under project risks, coordination
proved a major headache, and abssrbed unexpected quantities of staff time
particularly during project star--up. Though an Interagency Coordination
Comittee that was established sade some contribution, major problems still
arose. Frequently NHA had to take action to fund and execute critical off-
site works when a line agency cculd not carry out its responsibilities due
mainly to budget constraints. :his not only led to delays and excessive
141 NBA views its accounting practice as reasonably satisfactory.



137
demands on NRA management and staff, it also caused subsequent problems with
turnover of completed works. Agencies were reluctant to accept completed
components at the asset values shown as well as responsibility for maintenance
when they had not been directly involved with execution. Turnover and
maintenance problems are covered in paras 4.21 - 4.23. Though their impact
was financial, turnover delays were due in large measure to weak coordination
and construction delays which increased costs. Staff transitions in all
agencies and poor long-term institutional memory of previous agreements also
contributed. Under Urban II and III which continued development of the Dagat
Dagatan site and expanded both upgrading and serviced sites to regional
cities, NMA began to develop earlier and tighter liaison with line agencies
and utilities ultimately taking over works, as well as clearer contractual
covenants regarding the financial obligations of both parties. As a result,
lag time between completion of works and turnover is expected to drop
significantly under Urban III.
6.16      The Authority's role within the sector changed significantly over
the project period. At start-up it was the only national housing agency,
having been created through the amalgamation of several others, and bore sole
authority for policy, production, and regulatory functions, including land use
regulation. In 1978, however, the sector underwent major changes with the
creation of the Ministry of Human Settlements (MHS) and six other shelter
institutions. Sectoral policy and leadership was vested in MHS, and a major
new source of housing finance established through the "pag-IBIG" provident
fund, a mandatory savings plan similar to social security. The pag-IBIG funds
were utilised to establish a secondary mortgage market through the National
Home Mortgage Finance Corporation (NHMFC), in which NHA participation through
mortgage sales was negligible. The Authority was largely excluded from the
new source of housing finance. While serviced site and upgrading work do
figure prominently in the National Shelter Plan 1983-88 program by number of
units, by volume of total investment NHA plays only a modest role. It does
carry primary responsibility for serving the lowest income groups.
6.17      Perhaps in part because NHA predated the MHS system, the Authority
has never enjoyed particularly close coordination or working relations with
the Ministry or its agencies. The Authority has sold a few condominiums to
NHMFC, but has not until recently been able to tap the potential liquidity
from sales of its low income serviced site mortgage portfolio. A closer
working arrangement is now being undertaken to achieve this goal. As noted in
earlier sections of this report, competitive production functions have evolved
which have not contributed to the efficient use of resources. Establishment
of better, more effective relations between NHMFC and NBA has remained an
elusive goal, but one which the Government needs to rigorously pursue. There
are recent encouraging signs that together the two agencies can develop a
sound program of low income housing.
B. Ministry of Public Works and Highways (MPWH)
6.18      The traffic management component was well executed by the MPWH and
by their consultants. This component was implemented quickly, monitored well,
produced substantial operating cost and time savings, and has consequently
been well received by the public.



138
6.19      The Department of Highways and the Department of Public Works,
Transportation and Communications satisfactorily executed their responsi-
bilities for detailed design of roads/traffic engineering and a Metropolitan
Manila transport/land use study, respectively. Though large, the MPWH
functions reasonably efficiently in civil works design and construction.
Extensive delays and cost overruns encountered in the roads and bridges
component were not primarily due to institutional weaknesses, but to other
factors (para. 3.02). Detailed design, however, should have been further
along before appraisal.
C. Metropolitan Waterworks and Sewerage System (RWSS)
6.20      The MWSS' performance under the project was not entirely
satisfactory. The agency was several years behind schedule in supplying water
to Tondo and constructing the new outfall sewer. However, these problems have
now been resolved except for continuing low water pressure at Tondo and the
fact that the outfall sewer is not yet functional due to delays in completion
of a pumping station which is not Bank-financed. Pressure problems will be
remedied when MWS II is completed during 1986. Water and sewer systems have
been tested and formally transferred to fWSS under two Memoranda of Agreement
with NHA (see para. 4.22). However, MWSS is not yet able to take all of the
sewage flow from Tondo, and low water pressure has created blockages in the
sewerage system. Remedial works on the C-3 interceptor sewer and the pumping
station at Dagat-Dagatan were also delayed but have now been completed.
D. The Role of Tondo Community
6.21      One of the most noteworthy features of the project was the active
participation of the Tondo Community in the upgrading project. Community
consultation was initiated early in the project design stage. The Community
Relations and Information Office (CRIO) was one of the first organizational
units of the project office. Intensive efforts were devoted during the
start-up period to educating, involving and discussing proposed plans with
Tondo residents. The task was not easy. Tondo contains a large number of
diverse and politically active groups for which the project offered an
excellent target of debate. It became at times a political football. In
addition, fully 84 different barangays (local organizational units) existed in
the immediate and contiguous area, which were organized into a f"deration. A
Citizen's Committee with membership from the many different Tond, -rganiza-
tions was formed, and sectoral groups established to review particular
components. Information was disseminated through a monthly newsletter, a
speaker's bureau from among the community leaders, question/answer handouts,
as well as meetings and seminars. The NHA's General Manager and senior staff
actively participated in key grass roots meetings, especially in the early
years of 1974-76, during reblocking discussions, and during the 1984/85 cost
recovery drive.
6.22      Long years of neglect, indifference and unfulfilled promises had led
to considerable skepticism by residents about Government's credibility.
Meetings were often heated, but usually open and frank. The NHA's
relationship with the community over the years was and is complex: a mixture
of sincere desire for open consultation and trust, periodic coercion,



139
cooperation, affection, hostility, political maneuvering, fierce independence,
and patronage. To generalize about a ten year relationship with 180,000
people is difficult, but by and large the Authority appeared by 1983 to have
won the community's support. A 1983 move by MHS to take over more direct
jurisdiction of the Dagat-Dagatan and Tondo projects generated a fierce
defense of NHA by the community.
6.23      A new chapter in community relations with Tondo is just beginning as
collections get seriously underway. Some evidence is apparent of political
resistance to even the subsidized prices being charged, no doubt partly
attributable to the erosion in real income that must have resulted from the
50X inflation in 1984, and partly to the Authority's intense 1984 cost
recovery drive. These efforts should continue to be monitored closely as a
part of the suppervision of succeeding NIA projects (Urban II, III and IV).
E. Performance of Contractors and Consultants
6.24      Initially contractors did not perform well and NHA supervision was
inadequate. Poor contractor performance was a major contribution to delay in
the 1978-79 period. Infrastructure construction began to accelerate in 1980
as a result of the decision to terminate one large contractor, and to award
smaller contract packages to seven new contractors and also as the result of
increased efficiency in the design and reblocking procedures. The C-3
Interceptor Sewer in Dagat-Dagatan was delayed because the contractor
abandoned the contract and had to be replaced. Construction activity peaked
in both Tondo and Dagat-Dagatan in December 1980, slackening thereafter due in
part to lagging efforts by contractors who became discouraged by periodic
disruptions and obstructions by residents. In Old Tondo delays occurred in
the removal of structures obstructing street rights-of-way. The more serious
cases had to be taken to court and many were only settled in early 1982,
6.25      Principal causes of poor contractor performance included:
(a) inexperience on the part of contractors generally and especially in this
type of undertaking; (b) inappropriate packaging of some works which were too
large for effective management by local contractors; (c) community
disruptions; (d) resettlement delays, and (e) inexperienced contract
management and supervision by NHA. Toward the latter years, the Authority's
contract supervision improved relative to the initial years, and contractor
performance was adequate (see para. 4.06(e) for a discussion of NHA contract
supervision).
6.26      Both contractors and consultants performed well on the traffic
management component which was also well monitored. Consultants employed
under the other elements of the large rechnical assistance component performed
reasonably well, except for a few who had to be replaced. Consultants for
organization, management and financial planning at NBA performed adequately,
but the infusion of technical assistance did not achieve the degree of
advancement hoped for in NHA's overall financial performance and management.



140
F. Performance of the Bank
6.27      Appraisal of the project was thorough though somewhat naive, and
supervision was frequent, averaging 25 staff-weeks of field time per year from
1977 through 1984. This high coefficient is not unusual in first generation
urban projects. Total supervision was estimated at 56 staff-weeks at
appraisal and actually required 194, a significant increase of almost
fourfold. Reasons for this include the policy environment and the subject
matter itself - slum improvement, and self-help housing (sites and services)
which were difficult and new. The project began to modify Government's policy
of slum clearance and mass relocation to distant areas. It was also complex,
simultaneously addressing a range of problems of Metropolitan Manila, and
involving a large number of institutions.
6.28      Several features of Bank performance are noteworthy as lessons for
the future:
(a) Performance deficiencies: detailed design and engineering were not
completed prior to appraisal on any project component. For Tondo
this created particular problems with soil conditions, subsurface
conditions and landfill requirements. Though soil investigations
were carried out during preparation, they proved insufficiently
detailed. Bearing capacities in Tondo turned out to be as low as
200 psf. Excavation deeper than six feet required expensive sheet-
pile lining and extraordinary costs were incurred to stabilize
excavation bottoms. Implementation and supervision time increased
as a result. For the major roads and bridges component, the lack of
detailed designs proved a major contributing factor in subsequent
delays and cost overruns. These sorts of engineering deficiencies
are puzzling in an institution which has been financing civil works
for over 40 years;
(b) Bank staff turnover: from start-up in 1975 to 1979 a number of
different project officers were assigned to the project as well as a
variety of consultants. Inevitably each required a period of orien-
tation, and each brought a slightly different focus and emphasis to
the work. The consequently changing Bank signals to UHA did not
contribute to steady, focused implementation. Finally the Authority
requested greater stability in Bank staff assignments which were not
altered from 1979-1983;
(c) Inadequate briefing: the proliferation of project officers and
consultants noted above was usually combined with inadequate
preparation and briefing, forcing ERA to utilize time and staff
capacity to brief visitors, and to justify and explain past
decisions;
(d) Inexperience: the relative inexperience of Bank staff (and
consultants) in combination with the overall tendency toward
optimism led to some fairly naive expectations at appraisal about
implementation schedules in particular. Insufficient allowance was
made for unexpected glitches and for learning; and



141
(e) Imbalanced supervision: Bank supervision concentrated heavily for
many years on civil works with insufficient attention to "software"
features such as land acquisition, mortgage documentation, cost
recovery and financial management. Each of the latter proved a
difficult and complex task for which far more lead time should have
been allowed. With the exception of land acquisition, none of the
other critical software features were even reflected in the project
implementation schedule.
VII.  ECONOMIC RE-EVALUATION
7.01      Appraised and actual economic rates of return for project components
with quantifiable benefits are presented in Table VII-l below. The measure-
ment of economic benefits for the slum upgrading, residential sites and
services and commercial/industrial sites was based on the estimated increase
in property values brought about by the provision of infrastructure and other
services.
7.02      Tondo/Dagat-Dagatan.   At appraisal, the economic rate of return for
the Tondo upgrading and Dagat-Dagatan sites and services was estimated at
21%. In re-evaluating the benefits after completion, NHA calculated an
overall 172 return for this component using the same general methodology. For
the residential component, a 15X return was realized and for commercial/
industrial areas a 25% return (vs. 40% at appraisal). Appreciation in
property values has substantively kept pace with increasing costs. The
Authority's calculations were based on 1983 market values and reflect project
costs actually incurred, which were converted to 1983 prices by using actual
rates of inflation. Foreign costs have been shadow-priced at 1.2 and
unskilled labor at 0.8. Land for Tondo was priced at its opportunity cost.
(Details of the cost/benefit streams are on file.) The 17% return is somewhat
below the 21% expected at appraisal because the project took longer to
complete than expected, resulting in higher costs and a corresponding deferral
in benefits. In addition, the disposable areat particularly for comuercial/
industrial areas, declined. Appraisal land value estimates were also opti-
mistic, and economic fluctuations eroded eventual prices.
7.03      Major Roads and Bridges.   Benefits of the major roads component were
based on estimated savings in road transport costs of all diverted traffic
from heavily congested routes in the Central Business District to the project
roads. This component is not yet complete, and was the focal point of a
supplementary loan made in 1983. Although costs have increased from P 102
million at the time of appraisal to the present estimate of P 339 million, the
component is still expected to yield a satisfactory economic rate of return
(about the same as the appraisal estimate) when both the cost and benefit
escalations are taken into consideration. Traffic flows appear to be in line
with appraisal estimates, and increased costs would be offset by increased
benefits based on current prices of fuel and time savings at present wage
rates.



142
7.04      The weighted average rate of return for the Tondo Foreshore
Upgrading, Dagat-Dagatan serviced sites, and the major roads and bridges
components was estimated at appraisal to be 22X and at completion to be 18Z.
The factors of delay and consequent cost increases noted above largely explain
the 4% difference. Though slightly lower than expected, the 18S return is
fully satifactory.
7.05      Traffic (TEAM).  The traffic component produced an internal rate of
return of 29Z. None was calculated at appraisal. The traffic signal system
reduced traffic delays by 17% and increased peak hour travel speeds by 10,
while permitting traffic volumes to increase by 8X and 58% on various
routes. An estimated P35 million pesos per year are saved on fuel costs and a
roughly equivalent amount in travel time savings. Thus, about P70 (US$4)
million per annum is being realized in current savings. Thus, the traffic
component produced major benefits at low cost and, accordingly, is one of the
most successful traffic management components financed by the Bank to date.
Annexes E and F detail benefits on the TEAM component.

VIII. CONCLUSIONS
A. Achievement of Objectives
8.01      Virtually all the project's stated objectives (para 2.05) were
achieved except that of financial replicability. The upgrading of Tondo - one
of the largest and more turbulent slums in Asia - was a highly ambitious goal
that was successfully achieved in physical and social terms. The slum
community virtually transformed itself over ten years into an upwardly mobile
neighborhood without appearing to have evicted the truly poor. Tondo has
affirmed the assumption that if given security of tenure and basic services,



143
families will build their own housing, the quality of which has surpassed even
the most optimistic predictions. The new serviced sites/self help approach
has also been well received by households, and like upgrading, has proven
socially as well as physically successful.
8.02      Attitudinal changes seem to have also occurred, though they are more
difficult to measure objectively. Tondo residents have become property owners
with a stake in stability, and although still politically vocal, there is
evidence of a much higher level of civic consciousness and responsible
citizenship. Residents took an active role in upgrading and proved reliable
partnars. Internal resolution of conflicting neighborhood interests gave them
an opportunity to exert a degree of control over public decision making and
their own lives and coomunity that had never before occurred. The community
has accepted the concept that benefits should be paid for, and has accepted
long term financial obligations to do so. All this offers evidence of
fundamental, though subtle, changes in attitudes.
8.03      Both upgrading and serviced sites have proven more cost effective
than previous approaches. Even with substanial cost overruns, capital devel-
opment costs of P28,000 for an average lot (P20,000 net of administrative and
other markups) were still only one fourth of a typical contractor-built,
completed "low cost" house.
8.04      Pricing and cost recovery - the two key elements of financial
replicability - were the principal disappointments. The project's intention
was to demonstrate "full" cost recovery (meaning development costs net of
community facilities), in this case totalling about 82% of total development
costs. As noted in para 4.07, this objective was eroded by the premature
setting of the Tondo sale price several years before actual project comple-
tion, which reduced direct charges to one third of what they should have
been. Indirect charges under utility tarrifs as well as profits on commer-
cial/industrial units were not affected. Thus cost recovery was cut from 82%
at appraisal to 65%, of which only about half was direct.
8.05      Land in Tondo Foreshore was, in effect, given away at P5 per m2
making the replication of the Tondo model highly unlikely. Even in the Dagat-
Dagatan Phase I site, land prices were not reflective of real values, making
the replication of such a project infeasible without substantial downward
adjustments in infrastructure standards.
8.06      Collections have only been effectively underway for a year or two,
an insufficient period on which to base any significant conclusion. The
initial collection efficiency of 54% established in NHA's first year of
serious collection effort clearly requires improvement. The Authority has
formulated an action program to address this crucial issue.
8.07      Thus, even if priced right in subsequent projects, low income
mortgages have yet to be proven collectable and financially sound
investments. Until this occurs, there is little hope of attracting
significant private capital and scaling up into high volume production more
commensurate with need. However, the recent collection initiatives by NHA
give reason for optimism. Financial performance often lags physical



144
performance by substantial margins, and intensive follow-up is beginning to
yield improved performance in this vital aspect.
8.08      The project initiated Bank operations in the shelter sector in the
Philippines. Issues and objectives of subsequent Bank work in this sector
have expanded beyond the fairly specific objectives of this first
demonstration project.
B. Loan Covenants
8.09      Loan covenants were generally complied with (Annex D), with the
major exception of the pricing policy and cost recovery performance which
became highly politicized, and the failure to reimburse NHA for infrastructure
built on behalf of other Ministries.
C. Lessons
8.10      In addition to the observations noted in para 5.24 regarding Bank
performance, a number of lessons can be drawn from this first project which
should prove useful in future sectoral operations:
(a) Size of "demonstration" projects: As a demonstration, Tondo was
overly ambitious. A gargantuan and challenging project for even a
highly experienced agency, Tondo's upgrading quite overwhelmed the
NHA's fledgling capacities in this kind of new operation. Mistakes
were highly visible, politicized, large scale - and thus not easily
rectified. A smaller, less conepicuous target area would probably
not, however, have been politically acceptable. Pressures were
intense to "do something" about the living conditions in the
Foreshore and neither Government nor the NHA would have been
amenable to a less ambitious project;
(b) Land Acquisition: The protracted delays in expropriating land in
Tondo and Dagat Dagatan have cast serious doubts upon the viability
of any large scale, high volume housing program which must rely upon
expropriation as the primary vehicle of land acquisition. The
Authority is exploring the affordability impact of shifting to land
purchase at market values, limiting expropriation to only
exceptional cases of which upgrading would probably constitute the
majority. Higher land prices would have to be offsct by lower
infrastrucutre standards and costs. However, the standards utilized
for both Tondo and Dagat-Dagatan offer ample scope for reductions;
(c)  Premature Pricing:  The prices set in 1978 under PD1314 proved
premature though the decision was taken after much deliberation and
in collaboration with the Bank. Actual completion occurred only
four years later and at double the expected cost. The community
wanted and needed to know the approximate monthly charges they were
expected to pay. However, once set, these prices rigidified and
could not be adjusted to reflect final actual costs. As a result,
the NHA subsidized the project to the extent of about P-80 million
(US$7.5 million). This represented a fairly common quandary in the



145
Bank's first generation projects. The NHA is subsequently trying a
new approach to pricing by (i) indicatinS a tentative initial price
to the counity    (ii) jointly updating this price every few months
in collaboration with the community's representatives to establish
the flexibility of the price and explain reasons for price
adjustment; (iii) reducing completion time by tackling much smaller
sites (e.g. Zonal Improvement Program) which in turn should modify
price escalation, and, where this is not feasible (iv) pricing
larger sites in phases. With these measures the NHA hopes to avoid
the premature pricing problem;
(d) Pricing/Cross-subsidy: A number of charges which should have been
incorporated into sales prices were not, resulting in losses to VNI.
These costs will ultimately have to be either subsidized by govern-
ment grant infusions or written off from NHA's capital. The most
prominent among these include pre-development costs (feasibility
studies, survey, etc.), interest during construction, marketing and
sales documentation, general administration, maintenance of infra-
structure prior to turnover, and collections default allowances. A
revised pricing policy has been prepared by NHA identifying the full
range of costs incurred during project development - a proportion of
which were unexpected - and proposing recovery of most through l.t
sales.
A second major pricing lesson has been drawn from the disappointing
performance of cross-subsidies (para 4.04). Proposals are under
study for dropping cross-subsidy (though not differential pricing)
and pricing all units to at least fully cover actual development
costs;
(e)  Community Commitments:  Promises made by NHA to the Tondo community
to gain goodwill and cooperation proved expensive over the longer
haul. Specifically, the cotuitment that collections would not begin
until tenure (title) was delivered and all services were operational
in effect took the control of collection start-up out of NHA's own
hands and subjected it to the viscitudes of the court-dominated
expropriation process and to fluctuations in other agencies'
priorities. Though the Authority later regretted the practical
consequences of this promise, it would have been difficult to avoid
making this kind of statement under the conditions of hostility and
suspicion prevailing at the time of project startup;
(f) Contract Size/Supervision: Local contracting capabilities were
overestimated at appraisal, with all contracts grouped into one
overall package. The subsequent default of the contractor and
disaggregations of the package into smaller contracts was responsi-
ble for significant delays. Since then, contracts have been smaller
and better tailored to local capacities. Supervision was also
initially too loose, and there was laxity in the imposition of
penalties which resulted in unreasonable time extensions. While
numerous lessons have been learned and applied in tightening NHA's
contract supervision, scope still exists for further improvement;
(g) Turnover: The eventual difficulties NHA encountered in turning over
completed infrastructure point to inadequacies in interagency
coordination. The Memoranda of Agreement were signed well after the
completion of infrastructure (6/82 for KWSS and 12/84 for MPWH), and



146
did not prevent extensive dickering over asset values and ossociated
debt liabilities. Earlier and more explicit agreements were
needed. The legal agreements on Urban I were rather vague on the
subject of theme transfers. Under Urban III, loan covenants have
been tightened and are more explicit regarding the obligations of
MMC and MWSS in receiving the assets and debt obligation associated
with infrastructure developed by NHA and MPWH. These agreements my
not, however, prevent repeated difficulties, especially if costs
substantially exceed those anticipated at appraisal. Ideally the
parent ministry or agency should itself provide at least trunk
infrastructure. Practically, however, this may not be done in a
timely manner, and the lack of key infrastructure can obstruct the
sale/occupancy of a site. The price for NHA's independence in
infrastructure provision may turn out to be constant vigilance in
sustaining closer, more effective interagency cooperation;
(h) Core Units/Building Materials Loans: In contrast to initial
expectations, there was litle use of materials selvaged from
demolished shanties in constructing new dwellings in Dagat-
Dagatan. Most families purchased new materials. In addition,
households frequently tore down elements of the more complete core
units and altered the structure to better suit their personal
preferences. Toilets were frequently replaced as well. Thus
considerable initial savings could have been achieved by reducing
the core to an absolute minimum. This, in fact, has been done in
subsequent phases of both Dagat-Dagatan and the Pasig site. Build-
ing material loan amounts of P3,500 (US$330) were considered too
low, and the range and stock of materials supplied by NHA
inadequate. The length of time required to process a loan was also
considered by staff and borrowers to be excessive; and
(i)  Renter's Policy:  No policy was initially established regarding
renters, who turned out to constitute fully half of all Tondo
residents. Multiple occupancy of structures turned out to be much
higher than anticipated. The NHA came under considerable pressure
to treat renters eouitably by providing to them tenure opportuni-
ties. The Author.ty contemplated and rejected joint plot ownership
proposals, and ultimately ended up utilizingl5, large number of plots
in the Dagat-Dagatan site for Tondo renters.      These lots have
therefore not been available to meet new demand from growth and thus
to stem the spread of squatters. Instead they served to reduce
overcrowding in Tondo. An additional drawback is that room renters
are acknowledged to be poor affordability risks and thus more likely
to resell or default. There is also distinct likelihood of an
influx of "renters" prior to upgrading in order to jump the large
queue awaiting allocation of a serviced site. The NHA is now
formulating a policy on renters and contemplating the tradeoffs
between its desire to serve the total community and the urgent need
to meet demand from new growth, and recover costs.
15/ As of the time of writing, a total of 7,800 units in Dagat Dagatan have
been allocated, all of which have been for resettlement and a portion of
which have been for Tondo renters.



147
8.11      Many of the lessons of Urban I have already been incorporated into
lOA's procedures. Others raise complex issues involving difficult tradeoffs
and are under study. Overall, however, a great deal of learning has occurred
through this project which is relevant not just to lHA's future work, but to
that of other agencies involved in both the financing and production of
serviced sites and upgrading programs world-wide.

PHILI"Pl?3
NAXILA tIAN DIVELOPHT PIOJHCT (LOA     1282/1272 TV-PH)
PROJECT COFLETIOW REPORT
Housins Agencies in the Ptilippines
A.   Financial Asoncies
(1)  Hoam  Develoment Mutural Fund:  ND   ot "PACIBIc" is a mandatory
provident fund (refundable payroll taz) for all forml sector
employes and employers. About 851 of stipulated 'contributions"
*re passed on to the National Rom Mortgage Finance Corporation
(NMUPC) which in turn purchases primary mortgages granted to HDKW's
members from loan-originating instituions. MEWMC currently pays 121
interest to HDOF for use of its funds. This covers EDW's operating
expenses oad dividend payments on members accounts, currently 102.
(2)  National Rome Mortpae IFinance Corporations MMC is a secondary
mortgage institution which purchases first mortgages from loan-
originating institutions. NMIMC invests HDfP trust funds in short-
term time deposits and government securities, and floats bonds to
raise additional resources. Bonds are currently offered at 151.
The mortgages it purchases yield 91 and 161, depending on lending
criteria.
(3) Moms Financing Corporation: HFC insures all residential mortgages
purchased by MIC and housing construction loans. Other mortgages
can also be insured by HEC, but these represent only a small percen-
tage (perhaps 101) of home loans originated in the Philippines.
B.   Production Agencies
(1) National Housing Authority: A currently is the only government
agency engaged in volume production of low-income housing projects,
largely with World Bank loans and government equity and subsidy
contributions. Serviced sites and slum upgrading constitute most of
Mil's program, with some resettlement, new construction and somll
business loan activity. The agency produces over 17,000 units per
year. NHA originates long-term mortgages which to date the NHMPC
has not yet purchased.
(2) Human Settlements Development Corporation: HSDC is primarily
involved in the shelter sector through its wholly-owned subsidiary,
the BLISS Development Corporation, which constructs about 2,000
subsidized apartments and high- to medium-cost units per year. The
BLISS program was established to demonstrate to the private sector
that moderate cost housing could be profitable. The private sector
has responded by starting to build both moderate cost and mixed



160
income housing, crrently putting BLSS in direct com    tition with
the private sector.
(3) lltional lousinA Corwratio:: INC produces and warehouses building
wtorisrol    hbieb it **IIl to go"enmt agencies and private
developers. Currntly, NBC's mjor udertaking is the production of
"flexibomes", prefabricated units wbich the Covernnt hopes to
produce at low cost for low-income failies. NRC's goal is mas
production to reduco materials costs.
C.   Reulatory &genc
(1)   uman Settlements  egulatory Comissiont IsaC implemets all laws
and regulations relatinsg to housing development and construction,
including soning, land use and subdivision requirements. Until
recently, the regulatory process for approval of housing projects
was lengthy, but ISRC has successfully eut the processing time from
siz months to as little as two weeks. BSRC's most important accom-
plisbmect has been to revise the building code and subdivision
standards to mko thm more compatible with sites and services
projects, eabling private developers to legally penetrate lower
cost markets.
In addition to the above agencies which are directi.y under the
authority of MHS, the Government Service Insurance System (GSIS) and the
Social Security Systm (SS) offer mortgage loans to their members at low
interest rates and for smaller amounts than the RDHF-NHHC-HFC system. In
turn, they are reaching somewhat lower income groups than the [ES finance
system, though not as low as the beneficiaries of the National Housing
Authority's projects. The GSIS and SSS are not, however, major contributors
to shelter finance.

Mr. Yukinori Watanabe

U. S. A.
Dear Mr. Watanabe:
This has reference to the final draft of the Project Completion
Report (PCR) on Tondo Foreshore Project. Once again, I would like
to express my appreciation for giving us the opportunity to comment
on the draft report.
We have reviewed the above-mentioned report and we noted that
certain statements in the report which we previously proposed to be
changed or deleted in the 6rlier drafts of the PCR, have been
retained in this final draft.
In particular, I would like to reiterate our concern over
certain items which as discussed in the report, may undermine the
shelter program for marginal and low income families as a whole.
We propose that the treatment of the item be presented as follows:
1. Cost Recovery.(p._12 para 3.10)
Iniitially, cost recovery wasone of 'the weakest
aspects of project implementation because of the slow
start-up of collections in Tondo. Recent figures,
however, show considerable impruvemeEnt
2.  Profits from C/I Areas (p.23 Para 5.03)
In the review of the performance of the comnercial/
industrial areas, the case of the Vitas should be
considered. Although the sale of lots from this area was
included in the computation of project financial perform-
ance, the revenues from commercial areas do not include
the Vitas Industrial area.
QOuzon Memorial Elliptical Road, Ciliman, Ouezon Cty * Tel. Nos. 9461 to 66



ALS5EX M
Page 4
173
3.  Institutional Performance and DeveloMent (p.34 Para 6.17)
To dismiss the project's institutional objectives
as limited to building NHA's production capacity is
incorrect. The Tondo project was envisioned to be the
Model Project for the Authority's Slum Upgrading Program
throughout the country.
4. Cost Overruns (p. 19 Para 4.08)
The term cost overrun immediately implies poor
management on the part of the Authority but this is not
true in the case of the Tondo Foreshore Project. The
cost differential between actual project cost and esti-
mates in Staff Appraisal Report is due to several factors
identified as follows:
(a) for Tondo, delays in start-up, underestimated
quantiities for lan' fill and reclamation work
due to unforeseen jbsurface conditions and
obstructions; and the generally higher labor
and materials costs owing to major petroleum
increases.
(b) for Da at-Dagatan, land fOst was higher than
appraisal estimate; recima tion work was
higher and the construction of sewer system
increased from original estimates.
(c)  for roads and bridges, major delays in start-up.
(d) for several outfall increased -cope of work
for interceptor and watermain c ross the
C-2 highway.
EWe therefore, propose to substitute the term cost
variance for cost overrun. This term appropriately
describes the difference between the actual project
cost and the cost estimates in thn report s.nce these
two sets of figures (i.e., the actual cost and the
estimates) are not comparable.
The details of the above and some of our comments on other
matters are attached.
Thank you for your continuing support
Very 'ruly yours,
G. Vl TOBIAS
Maj Gen  AFP (get)
General Manager

Dear Mr. Watanabe:
RE: Project Completion Report on Philippines - Manila tlrban
revelppment Project (1.oan 1282/1272 - W'-PH)
Thank you for sending us a copy of the abovemertioned report and for
allowing us to give our comments.
Overall, WF think that the teport is very comrprehensive and has correctlyJ
analyzed the critical factors behind the project's perforrnance, a first
of its kind in the Philippines and in the entire Rank operations. It
contains many useful informatioA, for future Bank assistance to the
Philippines as well as to other developing countries. We observed that
in addition to the project's overall implementation experience, a
substantial portion has been devoted to NHA's institutional maturation
and to the project's cost recovery problem. Tie report in effect appears
to be audit/assessment Of the project as a whole. We would like,
ho6ever, to touch further on the following areas:
1. Project Preparatlon and ]7esirn
1'le recornize the ur'-ent need for the project especially during
the period of its inception.  In retrospect, hoever, we note that
the project's basic weakness appears to have been its inadequate
preparation which led to substantial time overruns. Many elements
of the work were not simply foreseen or were not adequately allowed
for in the schedule. The sequencing of work and orchestration of
clearance and settlerment wlere noz well ur.derstood (par. 406). Road
and other rights-of-way had not been fully cleared when construction
started. The poor soil conditions, flat terrain and absence of
drainage have not been adequately anticipated (par. 406 (c)) which
would later have a bearing on drainage conditions in adjacent towns.

The experimental nature of the project, as w11 as the sen-
sitive issues involved, e.g., relocation of squatters, necessitates
a mow thorough understandihg of local situations and culture.
Start-up difficulties and the considerable confusion which existed
duri:.:- inlitial years of implementation (par. 406 (f))could have been
avoided had there been adequate lead timw for intensive counity
camraign for the necessity of the project. It was reported that
dela-.-s wre encomtered because residents refused to be relocated.
Rela:edly, the beneficiaries could have been informed if not
allowed to participate in the determination of charges they were
supp:sed to pay prior to project start-up. This could have pre-
vente I the anxiety on the part of the community to know what price
they were supposed to pay for their lots (par. 5.08) which necessi-
tate_ the issuance of PD 1314. Also the problems on collection and
cost recovery being encountered by the proponent could have been
avoi;ed had there been enougb community campaign.
2. Maintenance of Project Assets
We recognize the nece3sity of adequate maintenance in order to
achieve the desired level of benefits and to avoid wastage in
fina.:iaJl resources. The problem should be given its proper  focus,
i.e., how this requirement of maintenance could be met. Participation
of beneficiaries should also be encouraged.
3. Project Delays
It would be worthwhile to know whether the project suffered
delays in the delivery of project benefits and to what extent if
ever, as a consequence of delayed project completion and turn-over.
Relatedly, the matter of disappointing cross-subsidy (par. 5.04)
can partly be attributed to the delayed completion. It would be like-
wise informative if time overrun per component could be documented.
4. Selection of Project Beneficiaries
The screening process and Friteria adopted in the selection of
pro,ect beneficiaries as well ai a profile of existing beneficiaries
cou'- be discussed. The criteria for priority allocation could be
usef-:' reference for similar projects in the future. Moreover, the
infcriation would be useful in determining whether the project has
real'y reached the poor squatter families.
5. Stat- .s of Awarding of Titles
The report could make mention of the latest status of awarding
of titles to beneficiaries.

We hope that our cc*ments will be helpful in the finalization of the PCR.

page 14 CHAPTER UII MJOR RESULTS
3.11 To render this component more effective and responsive
to the traffic needs of the area, supplemntal works
were authorized to be undortaken. At the time of wri-
ting. the original works have been completed while the
said supplemental works are expected to be completed
mid 1986. Although costs exc*ded the estimates during
appraisal, project impacts indicate that the roads and
bridges component have substantively fulfilled its
objectives. Original implementation scheduloe should
have been timed to allow relocation/res*ttl*ment of
*ffected improvements/dwellings. In retrospect. the
process of cl*aring the right-of-way could have in-
cluded measures to secure cleared areas before con-
struction could really take off.
page 14 CHAPTER IV PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION AND COST
B. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
4.03 With the delay in project implementation attributed
mainly to right-of-way problems. unrealistic encalation
cost projections which led to the termination of origi-
nal construction contracts and considering that the
original scope of work has increased by about 110%. the
roads and bridges component lagged substantially behind
the overall project, requiring nine years to complete.
While project performance was significantly behind app-
raisal estimates, ultimately the needed major roads and
bridge facilities became operational in time to provide
the needed access and relief to the worsening traffic
congestion in the area.
4.04 Roads and Bridges: Delay in the roads and bridge com-
ponent was largely traceable to lack of working area
and delayed implementation. The extended implement-
ation period for the roads and bridges component
could summarily be attributed to the following factors:
1. Termination of the original contracts bid in 7
December 1978 and finally approved March 20.
193S. Unrealistic escalation cost projections
and delayed implementation led to the termina-
tion of the contracts.
2. Lack of working area due to non-availability of
right-of-way. The social problem of resettling/
relocation of affected families hampered the
clearing of the right-of-way due to issuance of
numerous courtiinjunctions and restraining orders.
3. Expanded scope of work to include connecting roads
and bridges and other facilities to render the
